Language as the Foundation of Human Knowledge and Cognition
Author argues that language evolves through objects, meanings, and linguistic vessels, defining human cognition and distinguishing humans from animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three-stage process of language evolution (objects, meanings, linguistic vessels) and describe the reciprocal relationship between language and thinking.

Core Idea
Language evolves through objects, meanings, and vessels, forming the basis of human cognition and knowledge.

We often take language for granted, but this passage argues that language is the very structure of human cognition and knowledge. It traces how language develops from new objects and meanings, and explains why humans—and not animals—possess a faculty of speech that enables discovery and expression. Understanding this will deepen your appreciation of how Islamic scholars have historically linked language to epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three factors in language evolution
The author lists objects (أشياء), meanings (معاني), and linguistic vessels (أوعية لغوية) as the three factors. Notice how they are presented as a causal chain: objects lead to relationships, which produce new meanings, which require new vessels.
In Arabic, derivation (اشتقاق) creates new vessels; in English, composition (تركيب).
2
Understand the outcome of this process
The author states that this evolutionary process leads to the emergence of a new science or art. Think of how Islamic sciences like fiqh or usul al-fiqh developed terminology as new cases arose.
3
Recognize the role of words as symbols
Words are symbols that point to things. Even if we don't know the essence of an object, knowing its name guides us to discover it gradually. This is why language is the fundamental constituent of humanity.
The author contrasts this with animals, which lack this guided discovery.
4
Distinguish between speech (nutq) and clear expression (bayan)
Nutq includes animal sounds (as per Quranic verse on birds), but bayan is uniquely human because it is a product of thinking. Bayan involves organizing language to express intellectual, emotional, and social life.
This distinction shows that human language is not merely sound but carries rational content.
5
Grasp the reciprocal relationship between language and thinking
The author concludes that thinking formulates language, and language directs thinking. This mutual influence shows that language is both a tool of thought and shaped by thought.
This insight is foundational for Islamic epistemology, where clarity of expression is tied to sound reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن تطور اللغة وتجدّدها يبرز من خلال أمور ثلاثة هي: الأشياء والمعاني والأوعية اللغوية. فالأشياء تبدأ بالتطور، حيث تظهر في البيئة أشياء جديدة، تؤدي إلى ظهور علاقات جديدة وهذه العلاقات بدورها تقود إلى معان جديدة، كما تقوم المعاني الجديدة بالتطور إلى أوعية جديدة، وذلك من خلال عمليات الاشتقاق بالنسبة للغة العربية، أو التركيب بالنسبة إلى الإنجليزية، وجميع هذه العمليات التطورية والتجديدية تؤدي إلى ظهور علم جديد أو فن جديد. وتعد اللغة المقوم الأساسي لإنسانية الإنسان، ولذلك يُعرف بأنه كائن لغوي، يسمع ويقرأ ويفكر عن طريق اللغة والكلمات ليست بأكثر من رموز للأشياء. ومع أن الإنسان قد لا يعرف حقائق الأشياء التي يتعامل معها؛ إلا أن معرفته بأسمائها تدله عليها، فإذا به يكتشفها شيئاً فشيئاً. وتلك مزية الإنسان عن الحيوان. فالحيوان رغم امتلاكه أصواتاً محددة، ولكنها لا تتميز بهذه الميزات سالفة الذكر عند الإنسان إذ إن خاصية النطق عنده لا تميزه عن الحيوان فحسب، بل حتى في إطار النوع الإنساني أيضاً يكون التفاضل بسبب هذا النطق ومبدأ العلوم هو مبدأ النطق ذاته، حيث ينشأ عن النطق الاهتداء من المجهول إلى المعلوم. ولذلك فنؤكد أن الإنسان قد خلق ناطقاً مدركاً معبراً عما في ضميره، حيث إن المعارف جميعاً عبارة عن وضع أسماء لمسميات وتعريف معاني تلك الأسماء وتحديدها بغية نقل ما في الذهن إلى ذهن الغير. إذن فإن هذا النطق ليس مجرد إحداث صوت فالطير لديه مقدرة على النطق: ﴿وَوَرثَ سُلَيْمان داوُودَ وَقالَ يا أَيُّها النّاسُ عُلِّمْنَا مَنطِقَ الطَّيْرِ وَأُوتِينَا مِنْ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ إِنَّ هَذَا لَهُوَ الْفَضْلُ الْمُبِينُ﴾ [النمل: ١٦/٢٧] ولذلك فيجوز أن نقول نطق الطير، ونطق الحيوان ولكن لا تسيغ اللغة العربية إسناد البيان بمفهومه الخاص إلى حيوان أعجم. إذ البيان نتاج تفكير، والتفكير بدوره يعمل على تنظيم وصوغ هذه اللغة لتتسع لشتى مناحي الحياة العقلية، والوجدانية، والاجتماعية في صورة عبارة لغوية، وبذلك يمكن تحديد وجه العلاقة التبادلية بين التفكير الذي يصوغ اللغة، وبين اللغة التي توجّه التفكير.
The development and renewal of language emerge through three things: objects, meanings, and linguistic vessels. Objects begin to develop, as new objects appear in the environment, leading to the emergence of new relationships, and these relationships in turn lead to new meanings, just as new meanings evolve into new vessels, through processes of derivation in the case of Arabic, or composition in the case of English, and all these developmental and renewing processes lead to the emergence of a new science or a new art. Language is considered the fundamental constituent of human humanity, therefore he is known as a linguistic being; he hears, reads, and thinks through language, and words are nothing more than symbols for things. Even if man may not know the realities of the things he deals with, his knowledge of their names guides him to them, and thus he discovers them little by little. And that is man's advantage over the animal. The animal, despite possessing specific sounds, does not have these previously mentioned characteristics in man, for the faculty of speech in him does not distinguish him from the animal alone, but even within the human species, differentiation occurs due to this speech, and the principle of sciences is the principle of speech itself, as speech gives rise to guidance from the unknown to the known. Therefore we affirm that man was created speaking, perceiving, expressing what is in his conscience, since all knowledge consists of placing names for named things and defining the meanings of those names and specifying them in order to transfer what is in the mind to the mind of another. Thus, this speech is not merely producing a sound; birds have the ability to speak: "And Solomon inherited David, and he said, 'O people, we have been taught the speech of birds, and we have been given of all things; indeed this is the manifest bounty'" [Quran 27:16]. Therefore it is permissible to say the speech of birds and the speech of animals, but the Arabic language does not allow attributing bayan in its specific concept to a non-speaking animal. For bayan is a product of thinking, and thinking in turn works to organize and formulate this language to accommodate various aspects of intellectual, emotional, and social life in the form of a linguistic expression, and thus the reciprocal relationship between thinking that formulates language and language that directs thinking can be determined.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the nature and development of language and its role in human knowledge. The author begins by identifying three factors in language evolution: objects (اشياء), meanings (معاني), and linguistic vessels (اوعية لغوية). New objects in the environment create new relationships, which generate new meanings, and these meanings require new linguistic vessels through derivation (in Arabic) or composition (in English). This process leads to the emergence of new sciences or arts. The author then asserts that language is the fundamental constituent of humanity, defining humans as linguistic beings. Words are symbols for things, and even if humans do not know the realities of things, knowing names guides them to gradual discovery. This distinguishes humans from animals. Speech (نطق) is not merely sound production; birds have speech as indicated by the Quranic verse about Solomon being taught the speech of birds. However, Arabic does not attribute 'bayan' (clear expression) to non-speaking animals, because bayan is a product of thinking. Thinking organizes and formulates language to cover intellectual, emotional, and social life, establishing a reciprocal relationship: thinking formulates language, and language directs thinking. The passage thus lays groundwork for a theory of knowledge rooted in language and divine revelation.

النطق
البيان

Language is not just a tool; it is the very fabric of human cognition, evolving through objects, meanings, and vessels, and enabling the discovery of knowledge.

Critique of Rationalist Labeling
Author critiques the labeling of philosophers as rationalist, citing a contemporary philosopher.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the critique of labeling philosophers as 'rationalist' and explain why the author considers this labeling superficial and redundant.

Core Idea
Calling a philosopher 'rationalist' is like calling water 'water'—obvious and empty.

This passage attacks the common practice of branding thinkers as 'rationalist' or 'traditionalist'. The author argues that such labels are not only simplistic but actually distort the richness of Islamic intellectual history. By examining a contemporary philosopher's critique, we will see why the term 'rationalist' is vacuous when applied to any philosopher, and how poor translation exacerbates the problem.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the critique
The passage criticizes the trend of labeling philosophers with terms like 'rationalist tendency'. Find where the author calls this a 'shameful path' and a product of 'superficiality'.
2
Analyze the philosopher's redundancy argument
The quoted philosopher says describing a philosopher as rationalist is like describing water as water. Why? Because reason is the philosopher's only tool. Recognize that this makes the label meaningless—it adds no information.
3
Trace the problem to poor translation
The philosopher attributes the classificatory trend to poor translation of foreign terms into Arabic. See how the misuse of 'reason' across different contexts leads to vague concepts.
4
Evaluate the alternative framework
The author contrasts the binary 'reason vs. transmission' with Ibn Khaldun's classification and Ibn Taymiyya's harmonization. Understand that the Islamic tradition offers more nuanced categorizations.

Mī
Chapter One: Section One - Development of Epistemology
Which depicted the matter with this conflict and showed it in other than its true face, and brought that forth in a way unsurpassed in his comprehensive book (Dar' ta'arud al-'aql wa al-naql, or Muwafaqat sahih al-manqul li sarih al-ma'qul), and through this book he affirmed that he is a rationalist demonstrator, just as his book The Refutation of the Logicians affirmed that he is a theologian of the first rank.
Thus it becomes clear that depicting the course of disagreement among the ancients as a type of opposing trends between 'people of reason' and 'people of transmission', as much as it represents a path that led its follower into a great deal of exaggeration in intimidation and excess in delusion, actually confirms the soundness of the Khaldunian classification and its superiority over others.
Here I ask the kind reader to allow me to quote a text - despite its relative length - from one of the contemporary Islamic philosophers, in which he seems to respond to and redirect this shameful path of distributing titles, stamps, and tendencies to the outputs of human intellectual energies that appeared in this form in the latter half of the twentieth century CE, as he says in this context... The clearest thing that really draws attention in such a wave is what falls under the name of tendencies, until they have become almost like the spread of fever in the veins of this solid thought. In place of what is called the rational tendency, or what is called the mystical tendency, or the intuitive tendency, or the empirical tendency, and so on to the end of these terms that reflect only superficiality in handling and emptiness in content. And that, in most cases, is because the term 'reason' itself is used by many great and small pens alike with differing concepts and dimensions. This wave has gone so far in its course that it finally concentrated in the hands of a few writers and scholars in what they claim as 'the rationalist tendency' which they heap with praise and admiration, and pin hopes on for reforming the present and future state of Islamic or Arab thought as they prefer to call it. So if you examine their statements carefully, you will get nothing from them except one simple and naive concept connected to the rules of logic as Aristotle left them, despite the participation of some of them in proving its sterility and deficiency.
Some of the disciples of these people have followed the same pattern, burdening themselves excessively in proving a rationalist tendency in this philosopher or that, and worked to promote this trait until they deprived readers of reaping many of the intellectual fruits that the minds of the distinguished figures—who were the subject of these people's study—have produced.)(1)
This philosopher attributed that classificatory trend to the poor translation that occurred among some of those who transferred those terms from their foreign environment to the Arabic environment, so they did not acquire specific clear meanings in the language they were transferred to, and he followed that by saying:
... Describing a particular philosopher as having a rationalist tendency is akin to describing water as water, since it is obvious that the philosopher uses only his sole speculative crutch in his philosophy, which is reason, and there is no way to investigate a problem, treat an issue, or examine a truth except through reason. Rather, the matter is more dangerous and comprehensive than that, for rational activity is not limited to philosophical production, but extends to linguistic and scientific production in its specialized precise sense.
Such people who call a particular thought or a philosopher himself as having a rationalist tendency have, unfortunately, lost the complete vision of the tool of reason and its derivatives, and they have also been ignorant of the integrated cognitive entity as presented by Islam in His Noble Book.
Indeed, perusing the Noble Quran and reviewing the words related to the process of perception and thinking and what is associated with them shows us that the components of thought and perception constitute a device
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the common tendency to label philosophers as 'rationalist' or assign them to specific 'tendencies' (naza'at). The author begins by referencing Ibn Taymiyya's works, particularly *Dar' ta'arud al-'aql wa al-naql*, to show that he was both a rationalist demonstrator (burhani) and a theologian of the first rank, thus transcending simplistic binary classifications. The author then argues that depicting ancient scholarly disagreements as a clash between 'people of reason' and 'people of transmission' is an exaggeration and delusion, and that Ibn Khaldun's classification is more sound. The core of the passage is a quotation from a contemporary Islamic philosopher who criticizes the trend of labeling. This philosopher argues that the proliferation of terms like 'rational tendency', 'mystical tendency', 'intuitive tendency', etc., reflects superficiality and emptiness. He attributes this to the misuse of the term 'reason' itself, which is understood differently. He specifically criticizes the 'rationalist tendency' label, saying it reduces to a naive concept tied to Aristotelian logic. He further notes that describing a philosopher as rationalist is as redundant as calling water 'water', since all philosophers use reason. The philosopher also blames poor translation from foreign languages for the unclear meanings. Finally, the passage points to the Quran as presenting an integrated cognitive entity, implying that the Islamic conception of reason is broader and more comprehensive than the simplistic labels. The author uses this critique to defend a more nuanced view of Islamic intellectual history.

Key Terms
النزعة العقلانية (al-naz'a al-'aqlāniyya): In this text, used pejoratively to describe a superficial label applied to philosophers, implying a naive adherence to Aristotelian logic. [Note: Commonly used in modern Arabic philosophy as a neutral descriptor; here it is criticized as reductive.]
العقل (al-'aql): The rational faculty used by all thinkers, but its meaning shifts; in this passage, it is the sole tool of philosophical reflection, but its misuse leads to superficial categorization. [Note: In classical Islamic logic, 'aql is the faculty of reasoning; here the author emphasizes its universal application and warns against reductive definitions.]
التصنيف الخلدوني (al-taṣnīf al-khaldūnī): Ibn Khaldun's classification of sciences and intellectual trends, presented as superior to binary oppositions like 'rationalist vs. traditionalist'.
درء تعارض العقل والنقل (Dar' ta'āruḍ al-'aql wa al-naql): Ibn Taymiyya's work aiming to harmonize reason and revelation, cited here as evidence of his rational-demonstrative approach.

Key Takeaway
The labeling of philosophers as 'rationalist' is a reductive and redundant practice that obscures the richer, integrated view of reason found in Islamic thought.

The Linguistic Root of Ma'rifa
Author provides the linguistic root and meanings of 'arafa and ma'rifa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the root meanings of ma'rifa and parse the verb 'arafa's transitivity as analyzed by Sibawayh.

Core Idea
Ma'rifa stems from the Arabic root ʿ-r-f, meaning knowledge and recognition.

Understanding the linguistic root of ma'rifa is the first step in distinguishing its various usages across Islamic sciences. This passage from the coursebook defines the term by providing its root, synonyms, and a grammatical example from Sibawayh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the root and basic meaning
The passage defines al-'urf as the opposite of denial. Recognize that this points to the root meaning of recognition or knowledge.
Root letters: ع, ر, ف
2
Find the synonym for knowledge
Al-'irfan is equated with 'ilm (knowledge). Note that this establishes the core semantic field of the root.
3
Analyze verb transitivity
Sibawayh's example 'arraftu Zaidan' with tathqil shows that the verb takes two objects. Understand why this grammatical detail matters for the definition of ma'rifa as a verbal noun.
Two objects: the person known and the thing by which he is known.
4
Distinguish linguistic vs. technical usage
Note the shift alert: in Usul al-Fiqh, 'urf means custom, not recognition. Differentiating these is critical for proper understanding of Islamic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف المعرفة . العرف ضد النكر (٤) ، والعرفان هو العلم، وعرَّفه الأمر: أعلمه إياه، وعرفه بيته أي أعلمه بمكانه وعرفه به وسمه قال سيبويه : عرفته زيداً، فذهب إلى تعدية عرفت بالتثقيل إلى مفعولين (٥) .
Definition of knowledge. Al-'urf is the opposite of al-nukr (denial), and al-'irfan is knowledge. And he made it known: he informed him of it. And he made him know his house: he informed him of its location. And he made him know him and gave him a mark. Sibawayh said: 'I knew Zaid' (with tathqil), so he went to the transitive of 'arafa with tathqil to two objects.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines the word 'ma'rifa' linguistically. It begins by stating that 'urf is the opposite of nukr (denial). Then it explains that 'irfan is knowledge. It provides examples of how the verb 'arrafa (to make known) is used: informing someone about a matter, making someone know a house by informing them of its location, and making someone recognize another by a mark. Finally, it cites the grammarian Sibawayh, who analyzed the verb 'arafa in its doubled form (tathqil) as taking two objects, as in 'I made Zaid known (to someone)'. This linguistic analysis establishes the root meaning of ma'rifa as knowledge and recognition.

Key Terms
العرف
العرفان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
The linguistic root of ma'rifa is knowledge and recognition, confirmed by Sibawayh's grammatical analysis of the verb's transitivity.

?
Look up the root of the Qur'anic term 'ibādah and identify its basic meaning. How does its root inform its technical usage in fiqh?
?

Desire (Hawa) as an Epistemological Obstacle
Author warns against following desires as a major obstacle to objective knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic argument that following desires (hawa) obstructs objective knowledge and distinguish between evidence-based (bayyina) and desire-based subjectivity.

Core Idea
Desire (hawa) is not just a moral failing—it is the primary epistemological barrier to objective knowledge.

This passage from a modern work on Quranic epistemology combines Qur'anic verses to establish that personal desires undermine objectivity in knowledge. The author uses specific verses to show the gravity of following desires after receiving knowledge, and contrasts the one who relies on clear evidence (bayyina) with the one who follows desires. Understanding this distinction is crucial for anyone seeking rigorous knowledge in Islamic sciences, as intellectual training requires purifying oneself from subjective biases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The author claims that following hawa is a major obstacle to objectivity (mawdu'iyya). Find the verse that links following desires after knowledge to loss of Allah's protection (Ar-Ra'd 13:37).
2
Extract the contrast
In Muhammad 47:14, the Quran contrasts two types of people: one on a clear proof (bayyina) vs. one whose evil deeds are made pleasing and who follows desires. Identify which side represents objectivity and which represents subjectivity.
3
Trace the consequences
The story of Balaam (Al-A'raf 7:175-176) illustrates how desire leads to degradation and denial of signs. Note the metaphor of the dog panting as a picture of insatiable desire.
4
Understand the remedy
The verse from Al-Jathiya 45:18 commands adherence to the shari'ah and avoidance of desires of those who lack knowledge. The author concludes that self-purification (tazkiyah) is necessary.
5
Synthesize the argument
Explain how these verses build a case for the epistemological imperative of controlling desire. The obstacle is not just moral but intellectual; the pursuit of objectivity requires ethical discipline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني: الفصل الأول- أخلاق المعرفة ووحدتها
كما أنه حرمان من ولاية الله تعالى:
وَلَئِنِ اتَّبَعْتَ أَهْوَاءَهُمْ بَعْدَ مَا جَاءَكَ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ مَا لَكَ مِنَ اللهِ مِنْ وَلِيٍّ
ولا واق﴾ [الرعد: ٣٧/١٣].
وفرّق بوضوح بين صاحب البينة (الموضوعية) وصاحب الهوى (الذاتية): أَفَمَنْ كان عَلَى بَيْنَةٍ مِنْ رَبِّهِ كَمَنْ زَيَّنَ لَهُ سُوءُ عَمَلِهِ وَاتَّبَعُوا أَهْوَاءَهُمْ
[محمد: ١٤/٤٧].
المختلفة وحذر من اتباع الهوى في صوره من حب للدنيا، والوقوع في شرك التكذيب بآيات الله حين يضعف التواصل الإنساني مع عناصر الهداية الإلهية، أو ينعدم بسبب من عدم الإفادة من معطيات الوحي:
﴿وَاتْلُ عَلَيْهِمْ نَبَأَ الَّذِي آتَيْنَاهُ آيَاتِنا فَانْسَلَخَ مِنْهَا فَأَتْبَعَهُ الشَّيْطَانُ فَكانَ مِنَ الْغاوِينَ، وَلَوْ شِئْنَا لَرَفَعْنَاهُ بِها وَلَكِنَّهُ أَخْلَدَ إِلَى الْأَرْضِ وَاتَّبَعَ هَوَاهُ فَمَثَلُهُ كَمَثَلِ الْكَلْبِ إِنْ تَحْمِلْ عَلَيْهِ يَلْهَتْ أَوْ تَتْرُكْهُ يَلْهَثْ ذَلِكَ مَثَلُ الْقَوْمِ الَّذِينَ كَذَّبُوا بِآيَاتِنَا فَاقْصُصِ الْقَصَصَ لَعَلَّهُمْ يَتَفَكَّرُونَ ﴾ [الأعراف: ١٧٥/٧-١٧٦]. وحثّ على التزام الإطار المرجعي البين بعيداً عن الهـوى الـذي سببه الجهل
المعرفي:
ثُمَّ جَعَلْنَاكَ عَلَى شَرِيعَةٍ مِنَ الأَمْرِ فَاتَّبِعْهَا وَلَا تَتَّبِعْ أَهْوَاءَ الَّذِينَ لَا يَعْلَمُونَ [الجاثية: ١٨/٤٥].
وهذا يستلزم تربية المتعلم والباحث على تزكية النفس، حتى تتخلص من الهوى الذي يعدّ عائقاً ضخماً أمام (الموضوعية) التي يتطلبها طريق العلم، ومنهج التفكير العلمي.
The Second Section: The First Chapter - Ethics of Knowledge and Its Unity. Also, it is deprivation from the guardianship of Allah Almighty: 'And if you follow their desires after what has come to you of knowledge, you will have against Allah no protector or helper' [Ar-Ra'd 13:37]. And he clearly distinguished between the one who has clear evidence (objectivity) and the one who follows desire (subjectivity): 'Is he who is on a clear proof from his Lord like him for whom his evil deed is made pleasing and they follow their desires?' [Muhammad 47:14]. Various (forms) and he warned against following desires in its forms, such as love of the world, falling into the trap of denying Allah's verses when human connection with elements of divine guidance weakens or ceases due to not benefiting from the data of revelation: 'And recite to them the news of him to whom We gave Our signs, but he detached himself from them; so Satan pursued him, and he became of the deviators. And if We had willed, We could have raised him thereby, but he inclined to the earth and followed his desire. So his example is like that of the dog: if you drive him away, he pants, or if you leave him, he pants. That is the example of the people who denied Our signs. So relate the stories that perhaps they will give thought' [Al-A'raf 7:175-176]. And he urged adherence to the clear referential framework far from desire whose cause is intellectual ignorance: 'Then We put you on an ordained way concerning the matter; so follow it and do not follow the desires of those who do not know' [Al-Jathiya 45:18]. And this necessitates training the student and researcher in self-purification, so that they become free from desire which is a huge obstacle before (objectivity) required by the path of knowledge and the method of scientific thinking.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological warning about the danger of following personal desires (hawa) as a barrier to objective knowledge. The author begins by citing Quranic verses (Ar-Ra'd 13:37) that equate following desires after knowledge with deprivation from Allah's guardianship, establishing a severe spiritual consequence. Then, a distinction is made between the one who relies on clear evidence (bayyina) and the one who follows desires (Muhammad 47:14), linking desire to subjectivity. The author expands by listing various manifestations of desire—love of the world, denial of divine signs—and illustrates with the story of Balaam (Al-A'raf 7:175-176), where attachment to earthly desires leads to degradation. The remedy is adherence to the clear referential framework (shari'ah) and avoidance of ignorance-based desires (Al-Jathiya 45:18). The passage concludes that this requires tazkiyah (purification of the soul) to remove the overwhelming obstacle of desire from the path of science and scientific thinking. All examples given are Quranic and directly support the argument.

الموضوعية
البينة

Key Takeaway
The Quran persistently links desire (hawa) with the loss of objectivity, establishing that ethical purification is a prerequisite for sound knowledge.

Objectivity in Issuing Judgment
Author outlines objectivity in judgment using Quranic commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic principles that ground objectivity in scientific judgment and apply them to avoid bias.

Core Idea
Objectivity in judgment is a Quranic command, not a secular invention.

In this lesson, we explore the first aspect of objectivity as outlined in the source: objectivity in issuing judgment. The author grounds this in specific Quranic verses that command justice even against personal interests, biases, and enmity. These principles are presented as firm foundations of scientific research ethics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verses cited
Locate the three Quranic verses used: An-Nisa 4:135, Al-Ma'ida 5:8, and An-Nisa 4:58. Note how each commands justice in judgment.
2
Extract the key commands
From each verse, extract the specific command: 'be persistently standing in justice', 'do not follow desire', 'do not let hatred prevent justice', 'judge with justice'.
3
Apply to scientific judgment
Understand that these commands forbid familial, social, economic, or hatred-based bias in scientific research. The researcher must be objective.
4
Connect to research ethics
Recognize that 'adl (justice) is a foundational principle of Islamic ethics that applies to all judgments, including scientific ones.
This ties into the broader Islamic tradition of epistemology where justice is a prerequisite for valid knowledge.

الباب الثاني: الفصل الأول- أخلاق المعرفة ووحدتها
التزام الموضوعية وللموضوعية هنا مظهران هما:
أ الموضوعية في إصدار الحكم:
۱۰۷
وذلك أن الله تعالى وجّه بالتزام العدل في إصدار الأحكام المختلفة، ويندرج
فيها الحكم العلمي، بعيداً عن أي عصبية أسرية أو اجتماعية أو اقتصادية: يا أَيُّها الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا كُونُوا قَوَامِينَ بِالْقِسْطِ شُهَدَاءَ لِلَّهِ وَلَوْ عَلَى أَنْفُسِكُمْ أَوِ أو الْوالِدَيْنِ وَالأَقْرَبِينَ إِن يَكُنْ غَنِيّاً أَوْ فَقِيرًا فَاللَّهُ أَوْلَى بهما فَلا تَتَّبِعُوا الْهَوَى أَن تَعْدِلُوا وَإِن تَلْوُوا أَوْ تُعْرِضُوا فَإِنَّ اللهَ كان بما تَعْمَلُونَ خَبِيراً) [النساء: ١٣٥/٤].
كما لا يؤثر على الحكم عداوة من يصدر الحكم بحقه:
﴿وَلا يَجْرِمَنَّكُمْ شَنَآنُ قَوْمٍ عَلَى أَلَا تَعْدِلُوا اعْدِلُوا هُوَ أَقْرَبُ لِلتَّقْوَى
[المائدة: ٨/٥]
ودعا إلى التزام مبدأ العدل وقاعدته في كل حال
و... وَإِذا حَكَمْتُمْ بَيْنَ النَّاسِ أَنْ تَحْكُمُوا بِالْعَدْلِ﴾ [النساء: ٥٨/٤]. ويعد هذا الأدب قاعدة متينة من قواعد البحث العلمي المتصلة بأخلاق الباحث (العارف)
ب - الموضوعية في التعامل مع الآخر:
·
لقد ذكر القرآن الكريم أحوال أهل الكتاب واعترف بما لدى بعضهم من محاسن في المعرفة والسلوك والخلاف مع اليهودية المحرّفة لا يعني إسقاط السوء على جميع أتباعها : لَيْسُوا سَواءٌ مِنْ أَهْلِ الْكِتابِ أُمَّةٌ قائِمَةٌ يَتْلُونَ آيَاتِ اللَّهِ آنَاءَ اللَّيْلِ وَهُمْ يَسْجُدُونَ، يُؤْمِنُونَ بِاللهِ وَالْيَوْمِ الآخِرِ وَيَأْمُرُونَ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ وَيَنْهَوْنَ عَنِ الْمُنْكَرِ وَيُسارِعُونَ فِي الْخَيْرَاتِ وَأُولَئِكَ مِنَ الصَّالِحِينَ، وَمَا يَفْعَلُوا مِنْ خَيْرٍ فَلَنْ يُكَفَرُوهُ وَاللهُ عَلِيمٌ بِالْمُتَّقِينَ﴾ [آل عمران: ١١٣/٣-١١٥].
وصفة الأمانة كذلك يتحلى بها بعضهم، كما لا يلتزمها آخرون:
The Second Section: The First Chapter - Ethics of Knowledge and Its Unity. Adherence to objectivity, and for objectivity here there are two manifestations: A) Objectivity in issuing judgment: That Allah Almighty directed adherence to justice in issuing various judgments, and included in that is the scientific judgment, far from any familial, social, or economic partisanship: 'O you who have believed, be persistently standing firm in justice, witnesses for Allah, even if it be against yourselves or parents and relatives. Whether one is rich or poor, Allah is more worthy of both. So follow not [personal] inclination, lest you not be just. And if you distort [your testimony] or refuse [to give it], then indeed Allah is ever, with what you do, Acquainted' [An-Nisa 4:135]. Also, enmity towards the one against whom judgment is issued does not affect the judgment: 'And do not let the hatred of a people prevent you from being just. Be just; that is nearer to righteousness' [Al-Ma'ida 5:8]. And He called for adherence to the principle of justice and its rule in every situation: 'And when you judge between people, judge with justice' [An-Nisa 4:58]. And this etiquette is a firm foundation of scientific research rules connected to the ethics of the researcher (the knower). B) Objectivity in dealing with the other: Certainly, the Noble Quran mentioned the conditions of the People of the Book and acknowledged what some of them have of virtues in knowledge and conduct, and the disagreement with distorted Judaism does not mean casting evil upon all its followers: 'They are not [all] the same. Among the People of the Book is a community standing [in obedience], reciting the verses of Allah during the hours of the night and prostrating [in prayer]. They believe in Allah and the Last Day, and they enjoin what is right and forbid what is wrong and hasten to good deeds. And those are among the righteous. And whatever good they do - never will it be denied them. And Allah is Knowing of the righteous' [Al-Imran 3:113-115]. And the attribute of trustworthiness is also possessed by some of them, while others do not adhere to it.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines two manifestations of objectivity (al-mawdu'iyya) as derived from Quranic guidance. The first is objectivity in issuing judgment, which includes scientific judgment. The author cites three verses: An-Nisa 4:135 commands standing in justice even against oneself or relatives, warning against following desires (hawa) that lead to injustice. Al-Ma'ida 5:8 prohibits hatred from obstructing justice. An-Nisa 4:58 is a general command to judge with justice. These principles are described as firm foundations of scientific research ethics. The second manifestation is objectivity in dealing with others, illustrated by the Quran's fair treatment of the People of the Book. Despite theological differences, the Quran acknowledges the virtues of some of them in Al-Imran 3:113-115, praising their worship and good deeds. This shows that objectivity requires recognizing truth and goodness wherever found, without blanket condemnation. The passage underscores that scientific ethics require both fairness in judgment and fairness in assessing others.

الموضوعية
العدل
العصبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) An-Nisa 4:135
B) Al-Ma'ida 5:8
C) An-Nisa 4:58
D) Al-Imran 3:113-115

Key Takeaway
Objectivity in judgment requires setting aside personal biases, as commanded by the Quran.

Objectivity in Dealing with Others
Author illustrates objectivity in dealing with others using Quran's fair treatment of People of the Book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Quran's model of fair evaluation of others, even when they belong to opposing groups.

Core Idea
The Quran acknowledges virtue in the People of the Book, teaching us to evaluate individuals fairly.

The second aspect of objectivity concerns how we assess others, especially those we disagree with. The author uses the Quran's treatment of the People of the Book as a model. Despite theological differences, the Quran recognizes the good deeds and faith of some among them, demonstrating that objectivity requires fair evaluation without blanket condemnation.

Guided Path
1
Read the cited verses
Read Al-Imran 3:113-115 and note what virtues are acknowledged in the People of the Book: they recite Allah's verses, prostrate, believe in Allah and the Last Day, enjoin good, forbid evil, and hasten to good deeds.
2
Recognize the principle
Understand that disagreement in theology does not justify blanket condemnation; individuals are judged by their own actions.
3
Apply to scientific exchange
This principle encourages recognizing contributions from those with different worldviews, as long as they are truthful and beneficial. It prevents prejudice in academic or scholarly interactions.
4
Reflect on balance
The Quran also criticizes distortions, but still affirms individual virtue. This balance is key: critique of ideas does not necessitate personal denigration.

The Second Section: The First Chapter - Ethics of Knowledge and Its Unity. Adherence to objectivity, and for objectivity here there are two manifestations: A) Objectivity in issuing judgment: That Allah Almighty directed adherence to justice in issuing various judgments, and included in that is the scientific judgment, far from any familial, social, or economic partisanship: 'O you who have believed, be persistently standing firm in justice, witnesses for Allah, even if it be against yourselves or parents and relatives. Be just; that is nearer to righteousness' [Al-Ma'ida 5:8]. And He called for adherence to the principle of justice and its rule in every situation: 'And when you judge between people, judge with justice' [An-Nisa 4:58]. And this etiquette is a firm foundation of scientific research rules connected to the ethics of the researcher (the knower). B) Objectivity in dealing with the other: Certainly, the Noble Quran mentioned the conditions of the People of the Book and acknowledged what some of them have of virtues in knowledge and conduct, and the disagreement with distorted Judaism does not mean casting evil upon all its followers: 'They are not [all] the same. Among the People of the Book is a community standing [in obedience], reciting the verses of Allah during the hours of the night and prostrating [in prayer]. They believe in Allah and the Last Day, and they enjoin what is right and forbid what is wrong and hasten to good deeds. And those are among the righteous. And whatever good they do - never will it be denied them. And Allah is Knowing of the righteous' [Al-Imran 3:113-115]. And the attribute of trustworthiness is also possessed by some of them, while others do not adhere to it.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines two manifestations of objectivity (al-mawdu'iyya) as derived from Quranic guidance. The first is objectivity in issuing judgment, which includes scientific judgment. The author cites three verses: An-Nisa 4:135 commands standing in justice even against oneself or relatives, warning against following desires (hawa) that lead to injustice. Al-Ma'ida 5:8 prohibits hatred from obstructing justice. An-Nisa 4:58 is a general command to judge with justice. These principles are described as firm foundations of scientific research ethics. The second manifestation is objectivity in dealing with others, illustrated by the Quran's fair treatment of the People of the Book. Despite theological differences, the Quran acknowledges the virtues of some of them in Al-Imran 3:113-115, praising their worship and good deeds. This shows that objectivity requires recognizing truth and goodness wherever found, without blanket condemnation. The passage underscores that scientific ethics require both fairness in judgment and fairness in assessing others.

Key Terms
الموضوعية
العدل
أهل الكتاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Al-Imran 3:113-115 demonstrate about objectivity in dealing with others?
A) All People of the Book are disbelievers
B) Individual virtue exists even among those with different beliefs
C) Only Muslims can be righteous
D) Good deeds of non-Muslims are not recognized

Key Takeaway
Objectivity in dealing with others requires evaluating individuals based on their actions, not their group identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using Al-Imran 3:113-115, write a short analysis of how a contemporary Muslim scholar should evaluate a non-Muslim scholar's work in the field of ethics. What should be looked for?
?
Identify a historical example where a Muslim scholar objectively praised a non-Muslim's knowledge, and explain how this aligns with the Quranic principle.

beginner

The Quranic Critique of Conjecture (Zann)
Quranic critique of conjecture as foundation for knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quran's rejection of conjecture as a basis for knowledge and distinguish it from certain knowledge using the verses quoted.

Core Idea
The Quran denies the status of knowledge to anything built on mere conjecture.

The passage opens by negating any meaning of knowledge that rests on conjecture, citing Quran 45:24. It then describes how the People of the Book only have superficial knowledge based on 'amanī' (wishful thinking) arising from conjecture. This lesson unpacks the Quranic critique and its implications for how we evaluate knowledge claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key verse
Locate Sura al-Jathiya, verse 24 in the Arabic and translation. Note the author's use of it to establish that conjecture does not constitute knowledge.
2
Understand amanī
The author explains that the Quran calls the People of the Book's superficial knowledge 'amanī' (wishes), caused by conjecture. Recognize that amanī are unchecked fancies that seem like knowledge but are not.
3
Distinguish zann from 'ilm
The Quran contrasts zann (conjecture) with 'ilm (certain knowledge). Understand that in this framework, only 'ilm is valid for truth claims.
This distinction is foundational in Islamic epistemology.
4
Apply the principle
Consider a contemporary claim in Islamic theology or fiqh that relies on weak evidence. Determine whether it would be rejected under this Quranic principle.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني: الفصل الأول أخلاق المعرفة ووحدتها
ونفي أي معنى للمعرفة ما دام مبناها قائماً على الظن لا أكثر :
... وَما لَهُمْ بِذَلِكَ مِنْ عِلْمٍ إِنْ هُمْ إِلَّا يَظُنُّونَ ﴾ [الحانية: ٢٤/٤٥].
۱۰۹
وفي حديث القرآن عن أهل الكتاب ورد ما يفيد أن معرفتهم بالكتاب لـم تتجاوز الخواطر العابرة والرؤى السطحية التي ظاهرها العلم وباطنها من قبله العيب والجهل، وذلك ما يسميه القرآن (الأماني) التي سببها ظنهم: وَمِنْهُمْ أُمِّيون لا أُمِّيُّونَ لا يَعْلَمُونَ الْكِتابَ إِلَّا أَمَانِيَّ وَإِنْ هُمْ إِلَّا يَظُنُّونَ﴾ [البقرة: ۷۸/۲].
و الأمية لغة نسبة إلى (الأم) أو (الأمّة) ومن لا يقرأ ولا يكتب، والعيـي الجافي، والأمية مؤنث الأمي مصدر صناعي، معناه الغفلة أو الجهالة)). وفي الآية المشار إليها تُحمل (الأمية) على المعنيين: أميـة القـراءة والكتابة، وأمية عدم التدبر والتفهم لما يقرؤونه بسبب الغفلة والجهالة. وإلى هذا المعنى الأخير ذهب ابن عباس (۳). مع أن بعض المفسرين يرجح المعنى الأول(۳)، من يشكك في نسبة الثاني إلى ابن عباس (٤) . غير أن ذلك لا يغير من معنى شمول الأمية المعنيبي: الأمية الأبجدية والأمية الفقهية الحضارية، بدليل أن (سورة العلق) قد أعطت معنى القراءة مضمونا يتجاوز المدلول الأبجدي ليشمل القراءة الكونية، كما يظهر ذلك حال تدبر آياتها.
ومنهم
وفي نهي القرآن عن الظن المرجوح تربية للمتعلم على السعي نحو استخلاص النتائج العلمية بدقة، وعدم الركون إلى الظن المجرد، أو التخمين القائم على غير ما بحث جادّ مثابر يستقصي الموضوع المبحوث من جوانبه المختلفة كيما يخلص إلى النتائج اليقينية.

٦ - التحذير من تزييف المعرفة
ولذلك فقد جاء الخطاب إلى أهل الكتاب محذراً من أي تلبيس متعمد للمعرفة:
﴿وَلَا تَلْبِسُوا الْحَقَّ بِالْبَاطِلِ وَتَكْتُمُوا الْحَقَّ وَأَنتُمْ تَعْلَمُونَ ﴾ [البقرة: ٤٢/٢]. وخاطبهم بسؤال استنكاري:
يا أَهْلَ الْكِتابِ لِمَ تَلْبِسُونَ الْحَقِّ بِالْبَاطِلِ وَتَكْتُمُونَ الْحَقَّ وَأَنتُمْ تَعْلَمُونَ [آل عمران: ۷۱/۳].
وتوعدهم حين يعمدون إلى تحريف المعرفة بدوافع منفعية خالصة: فَوَيْلٌ لِلَّذِينَ يَكْتُبُونَ الْكِتابَ بِأَيْدِيهِمْ ثُمَّ يَقُولُونَ هَذَا مِنْ عِنْدِ اللهِ لِيَشْتَرُوا بِهِ ثَمَناً قَلِيلاً فَوَيْلٌ لَهُمْ مِمَّا كَتَبَتْ أَيْدِيهِمْ وَوَيْلٌ لَهُمْ مِمَّا يَكْسِبُونَ
[البقرة: ٧٩/٢].
وعد مسلك التزييف المعرفي عن سابق علم ومعرفة جُرماً أخلاقيًا مضاعفا : وَإِنَّ مِنْهُمْ لَفَرِيقاً يَلْوُونَ أَلْسِنَتَهُمْ بِالْكِتابِ لِتَحْسَبُوهُ مِنَ الْكِتابِ وَمَا هُوَ مِنَ الْكِتابِ وَيَقُولُونَ هُوَ مِنْ عِندِ اللهِ وَمَا هُوَ مِنْ عِندِ اللهِ وَيَقُولُونَ عَلَى اللهِ الْكَذِبَ وَهُمْ يَعْلَمُونَ ﴾ [آل عمران: ۷۸/۳].
وَاتَّبَعُوا مَا تَتْلُو الشَّيَاطِينُ عَلَى مُلْكِ سُلَيْمانَ وَمَا كَفَرَ سُلَيْمَانُ وَلَكِنَّ الشَّيَاطِينَ كَفَرُوا يُعَلِّمُونَ النّاسَ السِّحْرَ وَمَا أُنْزِلَ عَلَى الْمَلَكَيْنِ بِبَابِلَ هَارُوتَ وَمَارُوتَ
Chapter Two: First Section: Morals of Knowledge and Its Unity
And negation of any meaning of knowledge as long as its foundation is based on conjecture, no more:
... "And they have no knowledge thereof; they follow nothing but conjecture" [al-Jathiya: 45/24].
109
And in the Quran's discussion of the People of the Book, it is mentioned that their knowledge of the Book did not exceed passing thoughts and superficial views whose outward is knowledge but inward is defect and ignorance. That is what the Quran calls (amanī) [wishes] caused by their conjecture: "And among them are unlettered ones who do not know the Book except [from] wishful thinking, and they are only conjecturing" [al-Baqara: 78/2].
And ummiyya linguistically is a relative adjective to (umm) or (umma), and one who does not read or write, and the ignorant, coarse. And ummiyya is the feminine of ummi, a synthetic noun, meaning heedlessness or ignorance. In the aforementioned verse, (ummiyya) carries both meanings: illiteracy of reading and writing, and illiteracy of reflection and understanding of what they read due to heedlessness and ignorance. Ibn Abbas held this latter meaning. Although some exegetes prefer the first meaning, some doubt attributing the second to Ibn Abbas. However, that does not change that ummiyya encompasses both meanings: alphabetical illiteracy and civilizational fiqh illiteracy, evidenced by the fact that Sura al-Alaq gave the meaning of reading a content that goes beyond the literal meaning to include cosmic reading, as appears upon reflecting on its verses.
And among them
And in the Quran's prohibition of weak conjecture is an education for the learner to strive to extract scientific results precisely, and not to rely on bare conjecture or guesswork not based on serious, persistent research that investigates the subject from its various sides in order to arrive at certain results.

6 - Warning against falsification of knowledge
Therefore, the address came to the People of the Book warning against any deliberate mixing of knowledge:
"And do not mix the truth with falsehood and conceal the truth while you know it" [al-Baqara: 42/2]. And He addressed them with a disavowing question:
"O People of the Book, why do you mix the truth with falsehood and conceal the truth while you know?" [Al Imran: 71/3].
And He threatened them when they deliberately distort knowledge for purely selfish motives: "So woe to those who write the Book with their own hands and then say, 'This is from Allah,' to sell it for a small price. Woe to them for what their hands have written and woe to them for what they earn" [al-Baqara: 79/2].
And He counted the path of deliberate falsification of knowledge, with prior knowledge and awareness, as a doubled moral crime: "And indeed, among them is a party who twist their tongues concerning the Book that you might think it from the Book, but it is not from the Book. And they say, 'It is from Allah,' but it is not from Allah. And they speak untruth concerning Allah while they know" [Al Imran: 78/3].
"And they followed what the devils recited during the reign of Solomon. And Solomon did not disbelieve, but the devils disbelieved, teaching people magic and what was revealed to the two angels at Babylon, Harut and Marut."
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological admonition from a contemporary Quranic study. It begins by stressing that knowledge cannot be based on mere conjecture (zann), citing Quran 45:24. The author then explains that the People of the Book are described as having only superficial knowledge, which the Quran calls 'amanī' (wishes) arising from conjecture. The term 'ummiyya' (unlettered) is analyzed: it can mean both literal illiteracy and a failure to truly understand and reflect, as Ibn Abbas held. The author argues that Sura al-Alaq expands the concept of reading to include cosmic reading, so 'ummiyya' encompasses both alphabetical and civilizational illiteracy. The Quran's prohibition of weak conjecture trains the learner to seek precise, certain knowledge through rigorous research.

The second part (section 6) warns against the deliberate falsification of knowledge. The author quotes verses that forbid mixing truth with falsehood and concealing the truth (2:42, 3:71). Then verses threaten those who write scripture with their own hands and claim it is from Allah for worldly gain (2:79). Finally, twisting tongues to make falsehood appear as scripture is condemned as a doubled moral crime (3:78). The passage ends with a reference to those who followed what the devils taught about magic in the story of Solomon (2:102), implying that replacing true knowledge with falsehood leads to harm and disbelief.

The overall message is that knowledge must be sought with certainty, and deliberately corrupting it is a grave sin. The examples are all from Quranic stories about the People of the Book, emphasizing the ethical responsibility of scholars and knowledge seekers.

Key Terms
ظن
أماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the Quran call the People of the Book's superficial knowledge?
A) 'Ilm
B) Amani
C) Ummiyya
D) Talbis

Key Takeaway
The Quran denies the status of knowledge to anything based on mere conjecture; real knowledge must be certain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a contemporary argument in Islamic bioethics (e.g., on cloning) that relies on statistical probability (conjecture) rather than certain textual evidence. Explain why the Quranic critique would apply.
?
Identify a verse in the Quran other than those cited that contrasts zann with 'ilm. Discuss how that verse reinforces the author's point.

The Deeper Meaning of Ummiyya
Analysis of ummiyya as dual illiteracy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Quranic term 'ummiyya' as comprising both literal illiteracy and lack of reflective understanding.

Core Idea
Ummiyya is not just not reading; it is not reflecting deeply on what is read.

The passage delves into the meaning of ummiyya in Quran 2:78, showing that it encompasses both inability to read and an inability to truly understand. The author argues, following Ibn Abbas, that ummiyya includes a failure to reflect and grasp the deeper meanings of revelation. This lesson explores that twofold meaning and its implications for knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Locate the verse and the discussion
Find Quran 2:78 in the Arabic and read the author's analysis of ummiyya. Note the linguistic root: umm (mother) or umma.
2
Understand the two meanings
The author explains that ummiyya can mean illiteracy of reading/writing, and also heedlessness and ignorance that prevents deep understanding. Ibn Abbas held the latter view.
3
Connect to the broader Quranic context
The author points to Sura al-Alaq, which expands reading to include cosmic reading. This suggests that ummiyya also means failing to 'read' the universe as a sign of God.
4
Apply the concept
Consider how a person might be literate in Arabic but still be 'ummi' in the deeper sense if they do not reflect on the Quran's meanings.

6 - Warning against falsification of knowledge
Therefore, the address came to the People of the Book warning against any deliberate mixing of knowledge:
"And do not mix the truth with falsehood and conceal the truth while you know it" [al-Baqara: 42/2]. And He addressed them with a disavowing question:
"O People of the Book, why do you mix the truth with falsehood and conceal the truth while you know?" [Al Imran: 71/3].

Woe to them for what their hands have written and woe to them for what they earn" [al-Baqara: 79/2].
And He counted the path of deliberate falsification of knowledge, with prior knowledge and awareness, as a doubled moral crime: "And indeed, among them is a party who twist their tongues concerning the Book that you might think it from the Book, but it is not from the Book. And they say, 'It is from Allah,' but it is not from Allah. And they speak untruth concerning Allah while they know" [Al Imran: 78/3].
"And they followed what the devils recited during the reign of Solomon. And Solomon did not disbelieve, but the devils disbelieved, teaching people magic and what was revealed to the two angels at Babylon, Harut and Marut."
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological admonition from a contemporary Quranic study. It begins by stressing that knowledge cannot be based on mere conjecture (zann), citing Quran 45:24. The author then explains that the People of the Book are described as having only superficial knowledge, which the Quran calls 'amanī' (wishes) arising from conjecture. The term 'ummiyya' (unlettered) is analyzed: it can mean both literal illiteracy and a failure to truly understand and reflect, as Ibn Abbas held. The author argues that Sura al-Alaq expands the concept of reading to include cosmic reading, so 'ummiyya' encompasses both alphabetical and civilizational illiteracy. The Quran's prohibition of weak conjecture trains the learner to seek precise, certain knowledge through rigorous research.

The second part (section 6) warns against the deliberate falsification of knowledge. The author quotes verses that forbid mixing truth with falsehood and concealing the truth (2:42, 3:71). Then verses threaten those who write scripture with their own hands and claim it is from Allah for worldly gain (2:79). Finally, twisting tongues to make falsehood appear as scripture is condemned as a doubled moral crime (3:78). The passage ends with a reference to those who followed what the devils taught about magic in the story of Solomon (2:102), implying that replacing true knowledge with falsehood leads to harm and disbelief.

The overall message is that knowledge must be sought with certainty, and deliberately corrupting it is a grave sin. The examples are all from Quranic stories about the People of the Book, emphasizing the ethical responsibility of scholars and knowledge seekers.

Key Terms
أمية
أمي

C) Illiteracy and being a non-Arab
D) Heedlessness and following conjecture

Key Takeaway
Ummiyya is a comprehensive term covering both ignorance of the text and ignorance of its deeper meanings.

The Ethical Crime of Mixing Truth with Falsehood
Quranic warnings against mixing truth with falsehood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic verses condemning talbis (mixing truth with falsehood) and tazyif al-ma'rifa (falsification of knowledge) and explain why it is a doubled moral crime.

Core Idea
Deliberately mixing truth with falsehood for worldly gain is a grave sin in the Quran.

The passage lists several Quranic verses that warn against deliberately confusing truth and falsehood, concealing the truth, and fabricating scripture. The author emphasizes that such acts are especially heinous when committed by those who know the truth. This lesson examines the ethical implications for knowledge seekers.

Guided Path
1
Read the verses cited
Locate Quran 2:42, 3:71, 2:79, and 3:78 in the Arabic and translation. Note the key phrases: 'talbis' (mixing), 'kitman' (concealing), and 'yaltun' (twisting).
2
Understand the act of talbis
The author describes talbis as deliberate mixing of truth with falsehood. It is not accidental error but a conscious act.
3
Recognize the motivation
The verses mention worldly gain (thaman qalil) as the motive. This makes the act a moral crime.
4
Appreciate the doubled crime
The author notes that falsifying knowledge while knowing the truth is a doubled moral crime. This is stated in 3:78: 'They speak untruth concerning Allah while they know.'

الباب الثاني: الفصل الأول أخلاق المعرفة ووحدتها
ونفي أي معنى للمعرفة ما دام مبناها قائماً على الظن لا أكثر :
... وَما لَهُمْ بِذَلِكَ مِنْ عِلْمٍ إِنْ هُمْ إِلَّا يَظُنُّونَ ﴾ [الحانية: ٢٤/٤٥].
۱۰۹
وفي حديث القرآن عن أهل الكتاب ورد ما يفيد أن معرفتهم بالكتاب لـم تتجاوز الخواطر العابرة والرؤى السطحية التي ظاهرها العلم وباطنها من قبله العيب والجهل، وذلك ما يسميه القرآن (الأماني) التي سببها ظنهم: وَمِنْهُمْ أُمِّيون لا أُمِّيُّونَ لا يَعْلَمُونَ الْكِتابَ إِلَّا أَمَانِيَّ وَإِنْ هُمْ إِلَّا يَظُنُّونَ﴾ [البقرة: ۷۸/۲].
و الأمية لغة نسبة إلى (الأم) أو (الأمّة) ومن لا يقرأ ولا يكتب، والعيـي الجافي، والأمية مؤنث الأمي مصدر صناعي، معناه الغفلة أو الجهالة)). وفي الآية المشار إليها تُحمل (الأمية) على المعنيين: أميـة القـراءة والكتابة، وأمية عدم التدبر والتفهم لما يقرؤونه بسبب الغفلة والجهالة. وإلى هذا المعنى الأخير ذهب ابن عباس (۳). مع أن بعض المفسرين يرجح المعنى الأول(۳)، من يشكك في نسبة الثاني إلى ابن عباس (٤) . غير أن ذلك لا يغير من معنى شمول الأمية المعنيبي: الأمية الأبجدية والأمية الفقهية الحضارية، بدليل أن (سورة العلق) قد أعطت معنى القراءة مضمونا يتجاوز المدلول الأبجدي ليشمل القراءة الكونية، كما يظهر ذلك حال تدبر آياتها.
ومنهم
وفي نهي القرآن عن الظن المرجوح تربية للمتعلم على السعي نحو استخلاص النتائج العلمية بدقة، وعدم الركون إلى الظن المجرد، أو التخمين القائم على غير ما بحث جادّ مثابر يستقصي الموضوع المبحوث من جوانبه المختلفة كيما يخلص إلى النتائج اليقينية.

٦ - التحذير من تزييف المعرفة
ولذلك فقد جاء الخطاب إلى أهل الكتاب محذراً من أي تلبيس متعمد للمعرفة:
﴿وَلَا تَلْبِسُوا الْحَقَّ بِالْبَاطِلِ وَتَكْتُمُوا الْحَقَّ وَأَنتُمْ تَعْلَمُونَ ﴾ [البقرة: ٤٢/٢]. وخاطبهم بسؤال استنكاري:
يا أَهْلَ الْكِتابِ لِمَ تَلْبِسُونَ الْحَقِّ بِالْبَاطِلِ وَتَكْتُمُونَ الْحَقَّ وَأَنتُمْ تَعْلَمُونَ [آل عمران: ۷۱/۳].
وتوعدهم حين يعمدون إلى تحريف المعرفة بدوافع منفعية خالصة: فَوَيْلٌ لِلَّذِينَ يَكْتُبُونَ الْكِتابَ بِأَيْدِيهِمْ ثُمَّ يَقُولُونَ هَذَا مِنْ عِنْدِ اللهِ لِيَشْتَرُوا بِهِ ثَمَناً قَلِيلاً فَوَيْلٌ لَهُمْ مِمَّا كَتَبَتْ أَيْدِيهِمْ وَوَيْلٌ لَهُمْ مِمَّا يَكْسِبُونَ
[البقرة: ٧٩/٢].
وعد مسلك التزييف المعرفي عن سابق علم ومعرفة جُرماً أخلاقيًا مضاعفا : وَإِنَّ مِنْهُمْ لَفَرِيقاً يَلْوُونَ أَلْسِنَتَهُمْ بِالْكِتابِ لِتَحْسَبُوهُ مِنَ الْكِتابِ وَمَا هُوَ مِنَ الْكِتابِ وَيَقُولُونَ هُوَ مِنْ عِندِ اللهِ وَمَا هُوَ مِنْ عِندِ اللهِ وَيَقُولُونَ عَلَى اللهِ الْكَذِبَ وَهُمْ يَعْلَمُونَ ﴾ [آل عمران: ۷۸/۳].
وَاتَّبَعُوا مَا تَتْلُو الشَّيَاطِينُ عَلَى مُلْكِ سُلَيْمانَ وَمَا كَفَرَ سُلَيْمَانُ وَلَكِنَّ الشَّيَاطِينَ كَفَرُوا يُعَلِّمُونَ النّاسَ السِّحْرَ وَمَا أُنْزِلَ عَلَى الْمَلَكَيْنِ بِبَابِلَ هَارُوتَ وَمَارُوتَ
Chapter Two: First Section: Morals of Knowledge and Its Unity
And negation of any meaning of knowledge as long as its foundation is based on conjecture, no more:
... "And they have no knowledge thereof; they follow nothing but conjecture" [al-Jathiya: 45/24].
109
And in the Quran's discussion of the People of the Book, it is mentioned that their knowledge of the Book did not exceed passing thoughts and superficial views whose outward is knowledge but inward is defect and ignorance. That is what the Quran calls (amanī) [wishes] caused by their conjecture: "And among them are unlettered ones who do not know the Book except [from] wishful thinking, and they are only conjecturing" [al-Baqara: 78/2].
And ummiyya linguistically is a relative adjective to (umm) or (umma), and one who does not read or write, and the ignorant, coarse. And ummiyya is the feminine of ummi, a synthetic noun, meaning heedlessness or ignorance. In the aforementioned verse, (ummiyya) carries both meanings: illiteracy of reading and writing, and illiteracy of reflection and understanding of what they read due to heedlessness and ignorance. Ibn Abbas held this latter meaning. Although some exegetes prefer the first meaning, some doubt attributing the second to Ibn Abbas. However, that does not change that ummiyya encompasses both meanings: alphabetical illiteracy and civilizational fiqh illiteracy, evidenced by the fact that Sura al-Alaq gave the meaning of reading a content that goes beyond the literal meaning to include cosmic reading, as appears upon reflecting on its verses.
And among them
And in the Quran's prohibition of weak conjecture is an education for the learner to strive to extract scientific results precisely, and not to rely on bare conjecture or guesswork not based on serious, persistent research that investigates the subject from its various sides in order to arrive at certain results.

6 - Warning against falsification of knowledge
Therefore, the address came to the People of the Book warning against any deliberate mixing of knowledge:
"And do not mix the truth with falsehood and conceal the truth while you know it" [al-Baqara: 42/2]. And He addressed them with a disavowing question:
"O People of the Book, why do you mix the truth with falsehood and conceal the truth while you know?" [Al Imran: 71/3].
And He threatened them when they deliberately distort knowledge for purely selfish motives: "So woe to those who write the Book with their own hands and then say, 'This is from Allah,' to sell it for a small price. Woe to them for what their hands have written and woe to them for what they earn" [al-Baqara: 79/2].
And He counted the path of deliberate falsification of knowledge, with prior knowledge and awareness, as a doubled moral crime: "And indeed, among them is a party who twist their tongues concerning the Book that you might think it from the Book, but it is not from the Book. And they say, 'It is from Allah,' but it is not from Allah. And they speak untruth concerning Allah while they know" [Al Imran: 78/3].
"And they followed what the devils recited during the reign of Solomon. And Solomon did not disbelieve, but the devils disbelieved, teaching people magic and what was revealed to the two angels at Babylon, Harut and Marut."
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological admonition from a contemporary Quranic study. It begins by stressing that knowledge cannot be based on mere conjecture (zann), citing Quran 45:24. The author then explains that the People of the Book are described as having only superficial knowledge, which the Quran calls 'amanī' (wishes) arising from conjecture. The term 'ummiyya' (unlettered) is analyzed: it can mean both literal illiteracy and a failure to truly understand and reflect, as Ibn Abbas held. The author argues that Sura al-Alaq expands the concept of reading to include cosmic reading, so 'ummiyya' encompasses both alphabetical and civilizational illiteracy. The Quran's prohibition of weak conjecture trains the learner to seek precise, certain knowledge through rigorous research.

The second part (section 6) warns against the deliberate falsification of knowledge. The author quotes verses that forbid mixing truth with falsehood and concealing the truth (2:42, 3:71). Then verses threaten those who write scripture with their own hands and claim it is from Allah for worldly gain (2:79). Finally, twisting tongues to make falsehood appear as scripture is condemned as a doubled moral crime (3:78). The passage ends with a reference to those who followed what the devils taught about magic in the story of Solomon (2:102), implying that replacing true knowledge with falsehood leads to harm and disbelief.

The overall message is that knowledge must be sought with certainty, and deliberately corrupting it is a grave sin. The examples are all from Quranic stories about the People of the Book, emphasizing the ethical responsibility of scholars and knowledge seekers.

Four Quranic Principles for the Ethics of Knowledge
Author lists Quranic principles: humility, avoiding stubbornness, scientific method, linking phenomena to judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four ethical principles of knowledge taught in the Quran and support each with the specific verse cited by the author.

Core Idea
Islamic knowledge is inseparable from ethical conduct: humility, avoidance of arrogance, verification, and linking phenomena to judgments.

The Quran not only commands the pursuit of knowledge but also prescribes a moral framework for how to seek it. This passage from 'Nazariyya Quran' extracts four key principles from the Quranic text: humility in learning, rejection of vanity and stubbornness, commitment to verification (the scientific method), and the recognition that every phenomenon in the Quran is accompanied by a value judgment. Understanding these principles transforms knowledge acquisition from a mere intellectual exercise into a spiritual discipline.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first principle: humility
Find the verse about Cain and the crow (5:31) and the author's lesson that even a teacher can learn from a student. Note the supporting verses (17:85, 12:76) that remind us of the limits of human knowledge.
Humility here means being open to learning from anyone, regardless of status.
2
Identify the second principle: avoiding vanity and stubbornness
Locate the verses about nations who rejoiced in their own knowledge (40:83) and Korah's arrogance (28:78). Understand how 'cognitive stubbornness' (العِندية) blocks one from benefiting from divine guidance.
This principle warns against the delusion that one's knowledge is complete or superior.
3
Identify the third principle: commitment to the scientific method
Find verse 17:36 that forbids pursuing what one has no knowledge of. The author explains this requires deliberation, verification, and not simply transmitting others' claims.
This parallels the modern emphasis on evidence and validation.
4
Identify the fourth principle: phenomenon accompanied by judgment
Look at verse 4:26 as an example: after listing rulings on women, the Quran states that this is guidance and repentance. The principle is that the Quran never leaves a phenomenon without a clear positive or negative evaluation.
This helps readers understand the moral significance of each described phenomenon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني: الفصل الأول أخلاق المعرفة ووحدتها
خفيّة إلى أهمية التواضع في البحث والإفادة مما عند الآخرين أيا كانت مستوياتهم الخلقية أو المعرفية:
فَبَعَتَ اللهُ غُرَابًا يَبْحَثُ فِي الْأَرْضِ لِيُرِيَهُ كَيْفَ يُوارِي سَوْأَةَ أَخِيهِ قَالَ يا وَيْلَتَى أَعَجَزْتُ أَنْ أَكُونَ مِثْلَ هَذَا الْغُرَابِ فَأُوارِيَ سَوْأَةَ أَخِي فَأَصْبَحَ مِنَ النادِمِينَ ﴾ [المائدة: ٣١/٥].
وهنا يجب أن يستشعر المعلّم أنه لا يمتلك كل جوانب المعرفة وتفصيلاتها، فقد يوجد عند المتعلّم ما لا يوجد عنده، ولذا كان التواضع أدبا من آداب المعرفة يستوي أمامه الجميع. وصدق الله:
﴿وَما أُوتِيتُمْ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ إِلَّا قَلِيلاً) [الإسراء: ٨٥/١٧].
وَفَوْقَ كُلِّ ذِي عِلْمٍ عَلِيمٌ﴾ [يوسف: ٧٦/١٢].
۱۱- تجنب الغرور والعِندية:
وقد قص القرآن عن أقوام بعض الرسل هذه الصفة الذميمة التي يجب ألا يقع فيها طالب المعرفة:
فَلَمّا جَاءَتْهُمْ رُسُلُهُمْ بِالْبَيِّنَاتِ فَرِحُوا بِمَا عِنْدَهُمْ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ وَحَاقَ بِهِمْ ما كانُوا بِهِ يَسْتَهْزِئُونَ﴾ [غافر: ٨٣/٤٠].
وقال على لسان قارون قال إنما أُوتِيتُهُ عَلَى عِلْمٍ عِنْدِي أَوَلَمْ يَعْلَمْ أَنَّ اللَّهَ قَدْ أَهْلَكَ مِنْ قَبْلِهِ مِنَ الْقُرُون مَنْ هُوَ أَشَدُّ مِنْهُ قُوَّةً وَأَكْثَرُ جَمْعاً وَلا يُسْأَلُ عَنْ ذُنُوبِهِمُ الْمُجْرِمُونَ [القصص: ۷۸/۲۸].
إن ذلك أن التربية الإسلامية موصولة بالله تعالى، فهي ربانية يعني المصدر، كما هي ربانية الوجهة والمسلك.
إن العِندية المعرفية تشكل حجاباً كثيفاً يحول بين الإنسان المغرور بمعرفته وبين إدراك أهمية الانتفاع بالتشريع الذي ينظم علاقاته المختلفة، ويحرمه من بركة النماء العلمي الموصول بمواهب المعرفة، ويجعله عرضة لرياح أخطائه المدمرة لمقوماته الإنسانية.
۱۲ - التزام المنهج العلمي:
وقد شدّد القرآن الكريم في النهي عن الخوض فيما لم تمكن طرائق المعرفة الحسية والعقلية من الوقوف عنده:
﴿وَلا تَقْفُ مَا لَيْسَ لَكَ بِهِ عِلْمٌ إِنَّ السَّمْعَ وَالْبَصَرَ وَالْفُؤادَ كُلُّ أُولَئِكَ كانَ عَنْهُ مَسْؤُولاً [الإسراء: ٣٦/١٧].
وهذا يفيد ضرورة التأني في تلقي المعرفة، وعدم جوار التسرع في الحكم، أي إنه يعني تحقيق المعرفة، وعدم الاكتفاء بالنقل للمعرفة عن الآخرين فحسب.
۱۳ - الظاهرة مقرونة بالحكم:
إن القرآن إذ يسوق الظاهرة فإنه لا يترك القارئ في حيرة من أمره، لا يعرف الحكم على هذه الظاهرة بالسلب أو الإيجاب، بل نراه بعد أن يورد صفاتها يقرن ذلك بإصدار الحكم العلمي عليها، كما في مثل هذه الآية: ويُريدُ اللهُ لِيُبَيِّنَ لَكُمْ وَيَهْدِيَكُمْ سُنَنَ الَّذِينَ مِنْ قَبْلِكُمْ وَيَتُوبَ عَلَيْكُمْ وَاللهُ عَلِيمٌ حَكِيمٌ﴾ [النساء: ٢٦/٤].
فبعد أن أبان ما في الآيات من أحكام تتعلق بالنساء، أعقب ذلك بإصدار حكم موضوعي حاصله أنه بعد بيان هذه الأحكام بتلك الصورة فإن ذلك طريق الهداية والتوبة.
Chapter Two: Section One – Ethics of Knowledge and Its Unity.
Subtle to the importance of humility in research and benefiting from others, whatever their moral or cognitive levels:
Then Allah sent a crow scratching the ground to show him how to hide his brother's naked corpse. He said, 'Woe to me! Have I failed to be like this crow and hide my brother's naked corpse?' And he became of the regretful. [5:31]
Here the teacher must feel that he does not possess all aspects and details of knowledge; indeed, there may be found with the learner what he does not have. Therefore, humility is an etiquette of knowledge before which all are equal. Allah said truthfully:
And you have not been given of knowledge except a little. [17:85]
And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing. [12:76]
11 – Avoiding vanity and stubbornness:
And the Qur'an recounted of the peoples of some messengers this blameworthy trait which the seeker of knowledge must not fall into:
Then when their messengers came to them with clear proofs, they rejoiced in what they had of knowledge, and there enveloped them that at which they used to mock. [40:83]
And He said on the tongue of Korah: He said, 'I was only given it because of knowledge I have.' Did he not know that Allah had destroyed before him from among the generations those who were greater than him in power and more in accumulation? And the criminals will not be asked about their sins. [28:78]
Indeed, that is because Islamic education is connected to Allah Almighty, so it is divine in source, as it is divine in direction and path.
Indeed, cognitive stubbornness forms a thick veil that separates the man deluded by his knowledge from realizing the importance of benefiting from the legislation that regulates his various relationships, deprives him of the blessing of scientific growth connected to the gifts of knowledge, and makes him vulnerable to the winds of his destructive mistakes for his human components.
12 – Commitment to the scientific method:
And the Noble Qur'an strictly forbade delving into what sensory and intellectual methods of knowledge have not been able to ascertain:
And do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge. Indeed, the hearing, the sight, and the heart – all those will be questioned. [17:36]
This indicates the necessity of deliberation in receiving knowledge, and the impermissibility of haste in judgment. That is, it means verification of knowledge, and not being satisfied with merely transmitting knowledge from others.
13 – The phenomenon is accompanied by a judgment:
Indeed, when the Qur'an presents a phenomenon, it does not leave the reader in confusion, not knowing the judgment on this phenomenon as negative or positive. Rather, we see that after it mentions its attributes, it couples that with issuing a scientific verdict on it, as in this verse: And Allah wants to make clear to you and guide you to the ways of those before you and to accept your repentance. And Allah is Knowing, Wise. [4:26]
After it explained the rulings in the verses concerning women, it followed that by issuing an objective judgment, the gist of which is that after explaining these rulings in that manner, that is the path of guidance and repentance.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage from 'Nazariyya Quran' discusses the ethics of knowledge from a Quranic perspective. It begins by stressing the importance of humility (التواضع) in research, citing the story of Cain and the crow (Quran 5:31) to illustrate that even a lowly creature can teach a lesson. The author notes that a teacher must realize they do not possess all knowledge; students may have insights they lack, so humility is a universal etiquette. Quran 17:85 and 12:76 reinforce that human knowledge is limited. Point 11 warns against vanity and stubbornness (الغرور والعِندية), using verses about past nations who were deluded by their knowledge (40:83) and Korah's arrogance (28:78). The author explains that such cognitive stubbornness creates a veil, preventing one from benefiting from divine legislation and causing destruction. Point 12 emphasizes commitment to the scientific method (المنهج العلمي), citing 17:36 which forbids pursuing what one has no knowledge of. This requires deliberation, verification, and not just transmission. Finally, point 13 notes that the Quran always accompanies a phenomenon with a judgment (الظاهرة مقرونة بالحكم), giving the example of 4:26 where rulings on women are followed by a statement that this is guidance and repentance. The overall message is that Islamic knowledge is divinely sourced and oriented, and learners must embody these ethical principles.

Key Terms
الغرور
العِندية
المنهج العلمي
الظاهرة مقرونة بالحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) ﴿وَلا تَقْفُ مَا لَيْسَ لَكَ بِهِ عِلْمٌ﴾ [الإسراء: ٣٦]

Key Takeaway
The Quran provides a complete ethical framework for knowledge: humility before others, avoiding the arrogance of one's own learning, verifying claims before accepting them, and recognizing that every described phenomenon carries a clear moral judgment.

Cognitive Prejudice as a Barrier to Revelation
Author introduces intellectual stubbornness as a thick veil blocking benefit from divine law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cognitive prejudice ('indiyya) in a scholar's approach and explain its three consequences on benefiting from divine guidance.

Core Idea
Cognitive prejudice is a thick veil that blocks a person from the blessings of revelation and continuous knowledge growth.

In this passage, the author warns against intellectual stubbornness, which he calls al-'indiyya al-ma'rifiyya. This state of being deluded by one's own knowledge creates a barrier that prevents a person from truly benefiting from the divine legislation (shar') that organizes all relationships. The passage is a cautionary reminder for every seeker of knowledge: without humility, knowledge becomes a source of destruction rather than growth.

Guided Path
1
Define the key term
Locate the term 'al-'indiyya al-ma'rifiyya' in the Arabic passage. Understand that it refers to intellectual stubbornness or bias, where a person is content with his own knowledge and rejects external guidance, especially revelation.
This term is central to the author's warning.
2
Identify the metaphor
Notice the author uses 'thick veil' (hijaban kathifan) as a metaphor. What does this suggest about the effect of cognitive prejudice? It completely blocks perception, just as a physical veil blocks sight.
3
List the three consequences
The passage mentions three harms: (1) prevents realizing the importance of benefiting from legislation, (2) deprives of the blessing of continuous scientific growth, (3) exposes one to destructive errors. Write them down in order.
These consequences escalate from missing benefit to personal destruction.
4
Relate to Islamic ethics of knowledge
Connect this warning to Islamic principles such as sincerity (ikhlas) and humility (tawadu') in seeking knowledge. How does the passage reinforce the idea that knowledge without humility is dangerous?
Consider Qur'anic verses like 'And do not walk upon the earth exultantly' (17:37).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن العِندية المعرفية تشكل حجاباً كثيفاً يحول بين الإنسان المغرور بمعرفته وبين إدراك أهمية الانتفاع بالتشريع الذي ينظم علاقاته المختلفة، ويحرمه من بركة النماء العلمي الموصول بمواهب المعرفة، ويجعله عرضة لرياح أخطائه المدمرة لمقوماته الإنسانية.
Indeed, intellectual obstinacy forms a thick veil that prevents the person deluded by his knowledge from realizing the importance of benefiting from the legislation that organizes his various relationships, and deprives him of the blessing of continuous scientific growth connected to the gifts of knowledge, and makes him vulnerable to the winds of his errors that destroy his human components.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage defines the harmful effect of cognitive prejudice (al-'indiyya al-ma'rifiyya). The author begins by stating that such prejudice acts as a dense barrier. It separates a person who is conceited about his own knowledge from understanding the value of applying divine law (shar') to organize all his relationships. This deprivation extends to losing the blessing of continual intellectual development that comes from engaging with knowledge as a gift. Finally, it leaves him exposed to the destructive consequences of his own mistakes, which dismantle his humanity. The tone is cautionary, emphasizing the ethical dimension of acquiring knowledge. In Islamic tradition, this resonates with concepts like sincerity (ikhlas) and humility (tawadu') in seeking knowledge.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It causes one to become a scholar.
D) It is acceptable as long as one is correct.

Key Takeaway
Cognitive prejudice is a spiritual and intellectual disease that blocks a person from the guidance of revelation and the growth that comes from humble learning.
