The Law of Change in the Human Soul
Explains change as a psychological and social law necessitated by God's permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic basis for the law of change (taghyir) operating in the human self (nafs) and explain its necessity due to God's unique permanence.

Core Idea
Change in the soul is an existential law necessitated by God's unique permanence.

This passage presents change as a fundamental law governing the human soul and society, rooted in the very nature of creation. Because only God is eternal and unchanging, everything else—including our inner states and social conditions—is subject to transformation. The Quran explicitly ties this law to human agency, making it a key concept for understanding divine justice, personal responsibility, and social reform.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational claim
Locate the opening statement that the field of the soul operates according to the law of change. Note that this is presented as a necessary consequence of God's unique permanence.
The author uses 'يقوم' (operates) to indicate a fundamental principle.
2
Find the Quranic proof for the law
The passage cites Quran 8:53. Read the verse and understand its logical structure: God does not change a favor until the people change what is in themselves. This establishes human inner change as the condition for divine revaluation.
This is a conditional relationship: if people change inwardly, then God may alter their external condition.
3
Examine the educational implication
The author draws a pedagogical conclusion: change begins with the individual, then spreads to the community. No one should resign to a bad state as if it were fate.
This is the practical application of the Quranic principle for self-development.
4
Connect to divine justice and human accountability
Quran 3:165 attributes negative outcomes to the self's own actions, teaching self-criticism. The author notes that this training in accountability is a key purpose of the verse.
The passage emphasizes that humans tend to blame external factors but are reminded of their own role.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقوم ميدان الأنفس وفق قانون التغيير، بوصف التغيير مظهراً نفسياً واجتماعياً، يحتمه تفرد الخالق - سبحانه - بالديمومة والبقاء الخالد، مما يجعل سنتاً أخرى كالسببية والمدافعة والابتلاء والتسخير والاستخلاف تفضي جميعها إلى الحراك والتغير المستمرين. وقد صرّح القرآن الكريم بحتمية هذا التغير وسنيته الوجودية في ميدان الأنفس فقال: {ذلِكَ بِأَنَّ اللهَ لَمْ يَكُ مُغِيراً نِعْمَةً أَنْعَمَهَا عَلَى قَوْمٍ حَتَّى يُغَيِّرُوا ما بِأَنفُسِهِمْ} [الأنفال: ٥٣/٨]. وذلك يعني - من الناحية التربوية - أن عملية التغيير تبدأ من الفرد ثم الجماعة وفقاً لمعاني الاستقامة لبلوغ مستوى أفضل على درب الفضيلة، كما أنه لا يجوز الركون إلى حال السوء بحسبانها قدراً مقدوراً. وعزا أي تغيير سلبي يقع على الفرد أو الجماعة إلى النفس أولاً: {أوَ لَمّا أَصابَتْكُمْ مُصِيبَةٌ قَدْ أَصَبْتُمْ مِثْلَيْها قُلْتُمْ أَنَّى هَذَا قُلْ هُوَ مِنْ عِنْدِ أَنفُسِكُمْ} [آل عمران: ١٦٥/٣]. وفي هذا تربية على النقد الذاتي، وإصلاح الخلل الناشئ عن تقصير الذات ابتداء. ولعل تخصيص التغيير السلبي - في الآية – دون الإيجابي راجع إلى أن الإنسان لا يشكو في العادة إلا من السوء وأما ما يرجع عليه بالفائدة فإنه يفاخر بأنه من بنات أفكاره، وصنع يديه: {وَإِذا أَنْعَمْنَا عَلَى الإِنْسَانِ أَعْرَضَ وَنَأَى بِجانِبِهِ وَإِذَا مَسَّهُ الشَّرُّ فَذُو دُعاءٍ عَرِيض} [فصلت: ٥١/٤١]. {وَإِنَّا إِذا أَذَقْنَا الإِنسَانَ مِنَا رَحْمَةً فَرِحَ بِهَا وَإِن تُصِبْهُمْ سَيِّئَةٌ بِمَا قَدَّمَتْ أَيْدِيهِمْ فَإِنَّ الإِنْسانَ كَفُورٌ} [الشورى: ٤٨/٤٢] إن التغيير من حال الاستقامة إلى حال العوج أو العكس مرهون بإحداث السلوك على مستوى الفرد أو الجماعة على أي نحو كان. ومعلوم أن الإنسان لا يستقر على حال دون أن يحدث تغيير في حركته، سواء كان ذلك مسلكاً مرغوباً فيه، أو غير مرغوب. ويشير القرآن إلى التغيير الذي طرأ على قوم سبأ من جراء عدم رعايتهم لنعمة الله عليهم، وكيف أنها استحالت إلى نقمة: {ولَقَدْ كانَ لِسَبَأٍ فِي مَسْكَنِهِمْ آيَةٌ جَنَّتَانِ عَنْ يَمِينٍ وَشِمالٍ كُلُوا مِنْ رِزْقِ رَبِّكُمْ وَاشْكُرُوا لَهُ بَلْدَةً طَيِّبَةً وَرَبُّ غَفُورٍ، فَأَعْرَضُوا فَأَرْسَلْنَا عَلَيْهِمْ سَيْلَ الْعَرِمِ وَبَدَّلْناهُمْ بِجَنَّتَيْهِمْ جَنَّتَيْنِ...} [سبأ: ١٥-١٧]. وبمقابل هذا الأنموذج السيئ فإن أنموذج أمة الخيرية والوسطية والشهود الحضاري التي قاد دعوتها محمد - وقال الله تعالى عنها: {كُنتُمْ خَيْرَ أُمَّةٍ أُخْرِجَتْ لِلنَّاسِ تَأْمُرُونَ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ وَتَنْهَوْنَ عَنِ الْمُنْكَرِ وَتُؤْمِنُونَ بِاللهِ} [آل عمران: ١١٠/٣]
The field of the soul operates according to the law of change, describing change as a psychological and social manifestation, necessitated by the uniqueness of the Creator—glory be to Him—in permanence and eternal endurance, which makes other laws such as causality, mutual repulsion, trial, subjugation, and succession all lead to continuous movement and change. The Holy Quran has explicitly declared the inevitability of this change and its existential law-like nature in the field of the soul, saying: 'That is because Allah would not change a favor which He had bestowed upon a people until they change what is within themselves' [Al-Anfal 8:53]. That means—from an educational perspective—that the process of change begins with the individual then the community, according to the meanings of uprightness to attain a better level on the path of virtue, and that it is not permissible to acquiesce to a state of evil considering it as predestined fate. And He attributed any negative change that befalls an individual or community to the soul first: 'What, when a misfortune smites you—and you have smitten with twice the like of it—do you say, “How is this?” Say: “It is from your own souls”' [Aal-Imran 3:165]. In this is training in self-criticism and rectifying the defect arising from the failure of the self initially. Perhaps the specification of negative change—in the verse—without the positive is because man usually only complains about evil, and as for what benefits him, he boasts that it is of his own ideas and the product of his hands: 'And when We show favor to man, he turns away and withdraws to his side; but when evil touches him, he has recourse to abundant supplication' [Fussilat 41:51]. 'And when We make man taste of mercy, he rejoices in it; but if an evil befalls them for what their hands have sent ahead, then man is ungrateful' [Ash-Shura 42:48]. Change from a state of uprightness to a state of crookedness or vice versa is contingent upon the occurrence of behavior at the level of the individual or community in whatever manner. And it is known that man does not settle on a state without making a change in his movement, whether that is a desirable path or undesirable. And the Quran indicates the change that befell the people of Saba' due to their failure to maintain Allah's favor upon them, and how it turned into punishment: 'There was for [the tribe of] Saba' in their dwelling place a sign: two gardens on the right and on the left. [They were told], “Eat from the provision of your Lord and be grateful to Him. A good land [have you], and a forgiving Lord.” But they turned away, so We sent upon them the flood of the dam, and We replaced their two gardens with two gardens...' [Saba' 34:15-17]. And in contrast to this bad model is the model of the nation of goodness, moderation, and civilizational presence whose call was led by Muhammad—and Allah Almighty said about them: 'You are the best nation produced for mankind, enjoining what is right and forbidding what is wrong and believing in Allah' [Aal-Imran 3:110].
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and educational commentary on the Quranic law of change (taghyir) operating in the human self (anfus). It begins by stating that the field of the soul functions according to the law of change, which is both psychological and social. The necessity of change stems from the unique permanence of the Creator; everything else is subject to change. This leads to other laws—causality, mutual repulsion, trial, subjugation, and succession—all producing constant movement. The author then cites Quran 8:53 to prove that the change of divine favors is contingent upon people changing what is in themselves. From an educational viewpoint, change starts with the individual then the community, and no one should accept a bad state as fate. The next Quranic quote (3:165) attributes negative change to the self's own actions, teaching self-criticism. The author speculates that the verse specifies negative change because people only complain about evil, but boast about good. This is supported by Quran 41:51 and 42:48 showing human ingratitude. The passage then generalizes that any change—from good to bad or vice versa—results from behavior. It gives the example of the people of Saba' whose ingratitude led to punishment. Finally, it contrasts with the model of the best Muslim community (3:110) that enjoins good and forbids evil. The overall message is that human agency is central to both positive and negative change, and that change is an inescapable law in the soul's domain.

الأنفس

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary cause of negative change that befalls individuals or communities?
A) Divine predestination without human involvement
B) Their own actions in changing what is in their souls
C) External enemies or natural disasters
D) The will of the community leaders only

Key Takeaway
The Quran establishes that change in the human soul is a necessary law, and it is triggered by inner change, not by fate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the Quranic verses cited in this passage that support the law of change in the soul. Explain how each verse demonstrates the principle that change begins from within.
?
Reflect on the educational implication: why must change start with the individual before the community? Provide a real example from the life of the Prophet (peace be upon him) where individual transformation led to collective reform.

Commentary Notes
beginner
5 min
From Individual Reform to Collective Transformation
Contrasts two Quranic examples to show how societal change flows from inner change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the Quranic logic that societal change (positive or negative) originates in individual behavioral change, using the examples of Saba' and the best nation.

Core Idea
Societal change begins with the individual's inner transformation, then spreads to the community.

After establishing the law of change in the soul, the passage applies it to concrete historical examples from the Quran. The downfall of the people of Saba' and the rise of the Muslim Ummah both illustrate how collective outcomes are rooted in individual attitudes and actions. Understanding this chain helps us see why the Quran emphasizes self-reform as the starting point for any social change.

Guided Path
1
Recall the principle from Lesson 1
Remember that change in external conditions is contingent upon change within people's souls (Quran 8:53). This principle is now applied to two case studies.
2
Analyze the negative example: Saba'
The passage tells the story of the people of Saba' who had gardens, prosperity, and a forgiving Lord. They turned away (أعرضوا) from gratitude and became ungrateful. The consequence was the flood of the dam and replacement with bitter gardens. Their inward change (ingratitude) led to external punishment.
The turning point is 'أعرضوا' – they turned away. This is the inner change that triggered the loss of blessings.
3
Contrast with the positive example: the best nation
The Muslim community described in Quran 3:110 is called the best nation because of its actions: enjoining good, forbidding evil, and believing in Allah. This positive collective outcome also stems from the individual and communal commitment to change toward virtue.
The passage uses this contrast to show that both reward and punishment follow from human choices.
4
Derive the lesson on human agency
The author concludes that change—whether from good to bad or bad to good—is absolutely contingent upon behavior at the individual or community level. No change is random or purely external.
This reinforces the educational purpose: humans are responsible for their own transformation.

Self-Criticism and Change in the Quran
Author discusses Quranic verses on self-blame and change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic principle of self-criticism (al-naqd al-dhātī) and explain how it drives behavioral change, using examples from the passage.

Core Idea
Self-criticism is the starting point for all meaningful change.

This passage explores a key Quranic teaching: that misfortune often stems from our own actions, and the first step toward improvement is honest self-appraisal. The author unpacks verse 3:165 and related verses to show how the Quran cultivates self-criticism. Understanding this principle is essential for anyone seeking to apply Quranic wisdom to personal and communal transformation.

Guided Path
1
Locate the key verse on self-criticism
Find verse 3:165 in the Arabic passage. Notice how the verse attributes the disaster to 'yourselves' — this is the foundation for self-criticism.
The author explicitly says: 'In this is cultivation of self-criticism'.
2
Analyze why negative change is specified
The author notes that humans complain about evil but boast about good. Identify the verses (41:51, 42:48) that illustrate this human tendency. This explains why the Quran highlights negative change as a lesson.
3
Examine the example of Saba'
Read the story of the people of Saba' (34:15-17). Note how their neglect of gratitude led to a change from blessing to curse. This is a historical illustration of the principle.
Contrast with the positive example of the Ummah of Muhammad.
4
Identify the educational takeaway
The passage concludes that the goal of education is to facilitate change from undesirable to desirable behavior. Observe how the author connects the Quranic examples to a broader educational framework.
Consider how this relates to curriculum design in Islamic education.

أوَ لَمّا أَصابَتْكُمْ مُصِيبَةٌ قَدْ أَصَبْتُمْ مِثْلَيْها قُلْتُمْ أَنَّى هَذَا قُلْ هُوَ مِنْ عِنْدِ أَنفُسِكُمْ ﴾ [آل عمران: ١٦٥/٣] ﴾ وفي هذا تربية على النقد الذاتي، وإصلاح الخلل الناشئ عن تقصير الذات ابتداء. ولعل تخصيص التغيير السلبي - في الآية – دون الإيجابي راجع إلى أن الإنسان لا يشكو في العادة إلا من السوء وأما ما يرجع عليه بالفائدة فإنه يفاخر بأنه من بنات أفكاره، وصنع يديه : ﴿وَإِذا أَنْعَمْنَا عَلَى الإِنْسَانِ أَعْرَضَ وَنَأَى بِجانِبِهِ وَإِذَا مَسَّهُ الشَّرُّ فَذُو دُعاءٍ عَرِيض [فصلت: ٥١/٤١]. ﴿وَإِنَّا إِذا أَذَقْنَا الإِنسَانَ مِنَا رَحْمَةً فَرِحَ بِهَا وَإِن تُصِبْهُمْ سَيِّئَةٌ بِمَا قَدَّمَتْ أَيْدِيهِمْ فَإِنَّ الإِنسانَ كَفُورٌ﴾ [الشورى: ٤٨/٤٢] إن التغيير من حال الاستقامة إلى حال العوج أو العكس مرهون بإحداث السلوك على مستوى الفرد أو الجماعة على أي نحو كان. ومعلوم أن الإنسان لا يستقر على حال دون أن يحدث تغيير في حركته، سواء كان ذلك مسلكاً مرغوباً فيه، أو غير مرغوب. ويشير القرآن إلى التغيير الذي طرأ على قوم سبأ من جراء عدم رعايتهم لنعمة الله عليهم، وكيف أنها استحالت إلى نقمة: ولَقَدْ كانَ لِسَبَا فِي مَسْكَنِهِمْ آيَةٌ جَنِّتَانِ عَنْ يَمِينِ وَشِمال كُلُوا مِنْ رِزْقِ ربِّكُمْ وَاشْكُرُوا لَهُ بَلْدَةً طَيِّبَةً وَرَبِّ غَفُورٌ، فَأَعْرَضُوا فَأَرْسَلْنَا عَلَيْهِمْ سَيْلَ الْعَرِمِ وَبَدَّلْناهُمْ بِجَنتَيْهِمْ جَنتين... (سبأ: ١٥/٣٤ - ١٧]. وبمقابل هذا الأنموذج السيئ فإن أنموذج أمة الخيرية والوسطية والشهود الحضاري التي قاد دعوتها محمد - وقال الله تعالى عنها: كُنتُمْ خَيْرَ أُمَّةٍ أُخْرِجَتْ لِلنَّاسِ تَأْمُرُونَ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ وَتَنْهَوْنَ عَنِ الْمُنْكَرِ وَتُؤْمِنُونَ باللهِ) [آل عمران: ۱۱۰/٣] وَكَذَلِكَ جَعَلْنَاكُمْ أُمَّةً وَسَطاً لِتَكُونُوا شُهَدَاءَ عَلَى النّاسِ وَيَكُونَ الرَّسُولُ عَلَيْكُمْ شَهِيداً﴾ [البقرة: ١٤٣/٢]. هذه الأمة ما كانت لتحظى بهذا الشرف إلا حين وقع الانقلاب الإسلامي الشامل بقيادة - محمد له فأحالها من واقع جاهلي مطبق، إلى حال تحضر واستنارة، تغيرت تبعاً لذلك مسلكياتهم العقدية والفكرية والحياتية تغيراً يكاد يكون عاماً شاملاً. وعلى هذا فإن الهدف العام للتربية يصبح العمل على التغيير من حال السلوك غير المرغوب فيه إلى الحال المرغوبة على أن تقوم عناصر المنهج الأخرى من محتوى وطرائق ببلورة ذلك وتقويم مدى تحققه عملياً.
Chapter Two: Section Two - Field of Knowledge - The Unseen and the Witnessed. 'Or when a disaster struck you, although you had struck [them] with twice as much, you said, 'How is this?' Say, 'It is from yourselves.'' [Al-Imran 3:165]. In this is cultivation of self-criticism and correction of the defect arising from the inadequacy of the self initially. Perhaps the specification of negative change in the verse—rather than positive—is because man usually only complains of evil, while as for what brings him benefit, he boasts that it is from his own ideas and the work of his hands: 'And when We bestow favor upon man, he turns away and distances himself; and when evil touches him, he is full of prolonged supplication.' [Fussilat 41:51]. 'And when We give man a taste of mercy, he rejoices in it; but if evil befalls them because of what their hands have put forth, then indeed, man is ungrateful.' [Ash-Shura 42:48]. Change from a state of uprightness to a state of deviation or vice versa is contingent upon initiating behavior at the level of the individual or the group in whatever manner. It is known that man does not remain in one state without effecting a change in his movement, whether that be a desirable path or undesirable. The Quran points to the change that befell the people of Saba' as a result of their not caring for God's blessing upon them, and how it turned into a curse: 'There was for [the tribe of] Saba' in their dwelling place a sign: two gardens on the right and on the left. 'Eat from the provision of your Lord and be grateful to Him. A good land and a forgiving Lord.' But they turned away, so We sent upon them the flood of the dam and replaced their two gardens with two gardens...' [Saba' 34:15-17]. In contrast to this bad example is the example of the best nation, the middle nation, and the witnessing civilization led by Muhammad - and God Almighty said about them: 'You are the best nation produced [as an example] for mankind. You enjoin what is right and forbid what is wrong and believe in God.' [Al-Imran 3:110]. 'And thus We have made you a middle nation that you may be witnesses over the people and that the Messenger may be a witness over you.' [Al-Baqarah 2:143]. This nation would not have attained this honor except when the comprehensive Islamic revolution occurred under the leadership of Muhammad, transforming it from an overwhelming ignorance to a state of civilization and enlightenment; consequently their doctrinal, intellectual, and life behaviors changed in an almost total comprehensive change. Accordingly, the general goal of education becomes working to change from an undesired state of behavior to a desired state, with the other elements of the curriculum—content and methods—crystallizing that and evaluating the extent to which it is practically achieved.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and educational commentary on Quranic verses, focusing on the concept of change through self-criticism. The author begins by citing verse 3:165, which attributes a disaster to the people's own actions, interpreting this as a lesson in self-criticism and correcting internal shortcomings. The author notes that the verse specifically mentions negative change because humans typically complain only about evil, whereas they take credit for good. Further verses (41:51 and 42:48) reinforce human ingratitude. The author then generalizes: change from uprightness to deviation depends on behavior. The example of Saba' (34:15-17) illustrates how neglecting divine blessings led to punishment. Contrastingly, the Ummah of Muhammad (3:110, 2:143) achieved honor through a comprehensive Islamic revolution. The passage concludes that the aim of education is to facilitate change from undesirable to desirable behavior, using curricular elements to achieve and evaluate this change.

النقد الذاتي
التغيير
الاستقامة

A) Because negative change is more common than positive change.
B) Because humans typically only complain about evil, not good.
C) Because positive change is not mentioned in the Quran.
D) Because the verse is about punishment only.

Key Takeaway
Self-criticism, rooted in Quranic teaching, is the essential first step for any meaningful transformation, whether personal or communal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Using the principle of self-criticism from verse 3:165, write a short analysis of how a contemporary Muslim community could apply this to address a social problem (e.g., lack of productivity). Identify the 'disaster' and the internal factors to be corrected.

The Quranic Principle of Mutual Struggle
Author explains the Quranic principle of mutual struggle between truth and falsehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Quranic principle of mutual struggle (mudāfa'a) and cite its evidence from the Quran.

Core Idea
Struggle between truth and falsehood is a divine law that preserves the earth.

Have you ever wondered why Allah allows conflict and struggle in the world? This lesson explores a profound Quranic principle: mutual struggle (mudāfa'a) between opposing forces is not a sign of chaos, but a divine law that prevents corruption and preserves truth. We will unpack this concept and see how the Quran uses it to explain the necessity of struggle for the survival of righteousness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key Quranic term
Locate the term 'المدافعة' (mudāfa'a) in the Arabic passage. Notice that it is also expressed as 'التدافع'. Understand that it means mutual struggle or repelling, not necessarily violent conflict, but a push-and-pull between opposing forces.
The author distinguishes this from mere destruction; it is a purposeful struggle.
2
Extract the values involved
The passage states that mudāfa'a occurs between two sets of values: truth, goodness, justice on one side, and falsehood, evil, tyranny on the other. Write down these pairs. Recognize that this is a moral and spiritual conflict, not just physical.
3
Analyze the Quranic evidence
The author quotes two verses (2:251 and 22:40). Read the translations carefully. Both verses say that if Allah did not repel some people by means of others, corruption would spread or places of worship would be destroyed. Understand that the 'repelling' is Allah's law working through human agency.
The second verse mentions specific places of worship, showing that the struggle protects religious freedom.
4
Connect to sociological concepts
The author compares mudāfa'a to natural selection or survival of the fittest in sociology. Note the key difference: in the Quranic view, the struggle is not random but guided by divine wisdom to preserve truth and righteousness. This is a 'sunnah' (divine law) consistent across history.
This connection shows the principle is not just theological but observable in societies.
5
Summarize the purpose
According to the passage, the purpose of mutual struggle is to prevent the corruption of the earth (fasād al-arḍ) and to ensure the survival of truth and righteousness. In other words, it is a protective mechanism, not a destructive one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ - مظهر المدافعة:
والمدافعة المقصودة هنا هي المدافعة بين قيم الحق والخير والعدل وبين قيم الباطل والشر والجور، وتلك حتمية قائمة في ميدان الأنفس، كي تستقيم حياة العباد دون أن يطالها الفساد الذي يقضي على العمران:
﴿وَلَوْلا دَفْعُ اللَّهِ النَّاسَ بَعْضَهُمْ بِبَعْضٍ لَفَسَدَتِ الْأَرْضُ وَلَكِنَّ اللهَ ذُو فَضْلٍ عَلَى الْعَالَمِينَ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٥١/٢].
وَلَوْلا دَفْعُ اللَّهِ النَّاسَ بَعْضَهُمْ بِبَعْض لَهُدِّمَتْ صَوَامِعُ وَبِيَعٌ وَصَلَواتٌ وَمَساجِدُ يُذْكَرُ فِيها اسْمُ اللَّهِ كَثِيراً﴾ [الحج: ٤٠/٢٢].
إن مظهر المدافعة أو التدافع من السنن التي عبر عنها علماء الاجتماع بالانتخاب الطبيعي أو بقاء الأمثل. وذلك بإشارة الآية الكريمة إلى أن ما فطر عليه الناس من مدافعة بعضهم بعضاً عن الحق والمصلحة هو المانع من فساد الأرض. أي إنه سبب لبقاء الحق وبقاء الصلاح مما يدل على أن مظهر التدافع
4 - The Manifestation of Mutual Struggle: The mutual struggle intended here is the mutual struggle between the values of truth, goodness, and justice and between the values of falsehood, evil, and tyranny. This is an inevitability in the field of souls, so that the lives of servants may be upright without being touched by the corruption that destroys civilization: 'And if it were not for God's repelling the people, some by means of others, the earth would have become corrupted, but God is full of bounty to the worlds.' [Al-Baqarah 2:251]. 'And if it were not for God's repelling the people, some by means of others, there would have been demolished monasteries, churches, synagogues, and mosques in which the name of God is mentioned much.' [Al-Hajj 22:40]. Indeed, the manifestation of mutual struggle or repelling is one of the laws that sociologists have expressed as natural selection or survival of the fittest. That is by the indication of the noble verse that what people are disposed to by nature of mutual struggle for truth and interest is the preventer of corruption of the earth. That is, it is a cause for the survival of truth and the survival of righteousness, indicating that the manifestation of mutual struggle...
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of 'mudāfa'a' (mutual struggle) as a Quranic principle. The author defines it as the struggle between opposing values: truth, goodness, and justice versus falsehood, evil, and tyranny. This struggle is presented as an inevitable reality in the realm of souls, necessary to maintain uprightness and prevent corruption. Two Quranic verses are cited: 2:251 and 22:40, both emphasizing that God's repelling parts of people by others prevents corruption and destruction of places of worship. The author then analogizes this to the sociological concept of natural selection or survival of the fittest, interpreting the Quranic teaching that this innate mutual struggle for truth and common good is what prevents the corruption of the earth, thereby preserving truth and righteousness.

Key Terms
المدافعة
التدافع
السنن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the purpose of the mutual struggle between people?
A) To cause destruction and conflict.
B) To prevent corruption of the earth and preserve truth.
C) To eliminate all values of falsehood permanently.
D) To establish natural selection as a law of nature.

Key Takeaway
Mutual struggle (mudāfa'a) is a divine law that ensures the preservation of truth and prevents corruption, as evidenced by Quranic verses 2:251 and 22:40.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find another verse in the Quran that illustrates the principle of mudāfa'a (e.g., the story of Prophet Musa and Pharaoh). Write a short paragraph explaining how that example fits the pattern described in this lesson.
?
Consider a contemporary social issue (e.g., rise of false ideologies). Using the mudāfa'a framework, analyze how the struggle between truth and falsehood in that issue can be seen as a means to prevent corruption and preserve truth, according to the Quranic perspective.

Two Levels of Quranic Discourse on Civilizational Interaction
Author distinguishes two levels of Quranic discourse on civilizational interaction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two levels of Quranic discourse on civilizational interaction and apply this distinction to analyze contemporary educational philosophy debates.

Core Idea
The Quran has two stances on the other: principled recognition and contextual warning.

The Quranic position on civilizational interaction is not monolithic. This lesson unpacks a scholar's distinction between two levels of Quranic discourse: one affirming the other's right to exist and the other warning against specific hostile groups. Understanding this distinction is crucial for avoiding simplistic readings of Islamic texts on coexistence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two levels
Locate in the passage the description of the first level (recognition of the other) and the second level (informative about seduction).
The first level is found in the sentence beginning 'المستوى الأول: خطاب القرآن عن نظرته الذاتية...' and the second level begins 'المستوى الآخر: خطاب القرآن الإخباري عن الآخر...'.
2
Analyze the first level
Note how it recognizes the other's independent being and the limited goal of conflict (defensive). The key concept is 'حرابة' as repelling aggression, not eliminating unbelief.
3
Analyze the second level
Note its historical specificity to certain groups (e.g., People of the Book in the Prophet's time) and its purpose of informing about schemes, not a universal judgment.
The passage says 'هذا مقيد بفريق من الكافرين' indicating contextual limitation.
4
Synthesize the relationship
Understand that these levels are not contradictory but complementary: the first defines the principle, the second addresses immediate threats without altering the principle.
The author states they are two levels of one discourse, not mutually exclusive.
5
Apply to educational philosophy
See how the author uses the distinction to argue that Islamic education should be an interactive equal but shift to contestation when faced with hegemonic Western pragmatism.
The passage ends by linking this to the absence of Islamic educational philosophy from equal competition due to two reasons (cut off).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجيب أحد الباحثين على ذلك بتفريقه بين الصراع والتدافع، إذ التدافع تعددية، والتعددية هي السنة اللازبة والإسلام مع التعددية، ومع تعايش الحضارات، وبذلك فيمكن أن يكون هناك قاسم مشترك بين الإنسانية يتمثل في العلوم الطبيعية المحايدة، في حين تتمايز الحضارات في الفلسفات. ويذهب باحث آخر إلى أبعد من ذلك حيث يستخلص من فكرة ختم النبوة انتهاء فكرة الدورات الحضارية، التي تعني ولادتها ضعيفة، ثم تقوى وتشتد، ثم تضعف وتزول. ولكن لما كان الفارق الجوهري بين الإنسان والحضارة هو أن الإنسان قد يمرض فتتعرض حياته العضوية للتحلل، أما الحضارة فإن أصابها المرض فتحللها فكري نفسي، وهذا قابل للعلاج والزيادة والنمو. ولذلك فإن أرنولد توينبي حينما تناول هذا الموضوع لم يحسم الأمر بانتهاء دورة حضارية لتحل أخرى محلّها، ولكنه تناول الأمر بقدر من التردد. ويختم هذا الباحث وجهة نظره بالتأكيد على أن فكرة ختم النبوة بالنسبة للرسول محمد - صلى الله عليه وسلم - للناس كافة؛ تعني إمكان إحلال فكرة عالمية الحضارة، وانتهاء زمن تعدد الحضارات. ومع أننا ما نزال نعيش دورة الحضارة وتعددها إلا أن إرهاصات زوالها بدأت تبرز لمن تأمل. ولعلّ الرأي الذي أخلص إليه بعد كل ما تقدّم أنه يجب التفريق بين مستويين من خطاب القرآن لمظهر التدافع الحضاري وذلك على النحو التالي: المستوى الأول: خطاب القرآن عن نظرته الذاتية إلى هذه السنة من منطلق الاعتراف الكامل بذاتية الآخر، وكينونته المستقلة، وحقه في إقامة الحضارة التي يتمحض اختياره المطلق في إقامتها، شريطة عدم تعديها لحدودها، أو تجاوزها إلى حقوق الآخر وحدوده. وفي هذا المستوى من الخطاب يتحدد موقف القرآن من الآخر على نحو جليّ من خلال تلك الآيات التي أوضحت أن صراع الإسلام مع الكفر ليس بهدف القضاء على هذا الكفر، وإنما بهدف ردّ العدوان الذي يُهدِّدُ به معسكر الكفر، فيما يسمى فقهياً بالحرابة. وقد أكد هذا موقف الإسلام العملي من خلال دعوة النبي - صلى الله عليه وسلم – في مختلف أطوارها. المستوى الآخر: خطاب القرآن الإخباري عن الآخر في حرصه على إغواء المسلمين بشتى الوسائل، وإصراره على ذلك. ولكن هذا مقيد بفريق من الكافرين، أو حتى بجملة الكافرين من أهل الكتاب على وجه التحديد في زمن النبي - صلى الله عليه وسلم - دون أن يعني ذلك استغراقاً لكل المخالفين بالضرورة، ودون أن يعني ذلك موقفاً يرتضيه الإسلام كذلك. فكأن الإسلام – بعد أن حدد موقفه من قضية التدافع الحضاري في المستوى الأول – يومى بأن الآخر يختلف عنـه في الموقف ذاته، ولذلك فإن صح أن تتحتم فكرة المدافعة الحضارية فإن المتسبب الأوحد فيها هو الطرف الآخر، لا الإسلام، وبناءً على هذا الاستخلاص فإن التربية الإسلامية لا تقف من الأفكار والاتجاهات موقف المناهض، وإنما موقف الندّ المتفاعل، ولكن حين تسعى بعض تلك الأفكار والاتجاهات الغربية - مثلاً - إلى الهيمنة ومحاولة بسط النفوذ على ما عداها من النظريات والأفكار والاتجاهات الأخرى، كما هو حال ما يسمى اليوم بالتربية الحديثة التربية البراجماتية) بحجة أن ما عداها رجعية وطوباوية عتيقة، حينئذ ينتقل موقف التربية الإسلامية من وضعية الإقرار والتعايش مع الآخر إلى موقف المنازع حول الأحقية والمشروعية وسمة العالمية. وهذا ما يفترض أن يكون انعكاسه واضحاً مباشراً عبر الفلسفة التربوية الموجهة للنظام التربوي بكل عناصره في المجتمع المسلم. ويبدو أن غياب فلسفة التربية الإسلامية عن ميدان التنافس المتكافئ يرجع إلى سببين رئيسين:
One researcher responds to that by distinguishing between conflict and push-and-pull, for push-and-pull is pluralism, and pluralism is the binding custom, and Islam is with pluralism and with coexistence of civilizations, and thus there can be a common denominator among humanity represented by neutral natural sciences, while civilizations differ in philosophies. Another researcher goes further, deriving from the idea of the seal of prophethood the end of the idea of civilizational cycles, which means their birth is weak, then they strengthen and intensify, then they weaken and vanish. But since the essential difference between man and civilization is that man may become sick so his organic life is subject to dissolution, while civilization if it becomes sick, its dissolution is intellectual-psychological, and this is treatable, increasable, and growable. Therefore Arnold Toynbee, when he addressed this topic, did not settle the matter with the end of a civilizational cycle to be replaced by another, but addressed it with a degree of hesitation. This researcher concludes his viewpoint by emphasizing that the idea of the seal of prophethood for the Messenger Muhammad - peace be upon him - for all people means the possibility of establishing the idea of the universality of civilization and the end of the time of multiple civilizations. And although we are still living the cycle of civilization and its multiplicity, signs of its demise have begun to appear for those who reflect. Perhaps the opinion I conclude after all that is that one must distinguish between two levels of the Quranic discourse concerning the manifestation of civilizational push-and-pull, as follows: The first level: The Quran's discourse about its own view of this custom from the standpoint of full recognition of the other's selfhood, independent being, and right to establish the civilization that his absolute choice is dedicated to establishing, provided that it does not transgress its limits or exceed into the rights and limits of the other. In this level of discourse, the Quran's stance toward the other is clearly determined through those verses that clarified that Islam's conflict with unbelief is not for the purpose of eliminating this unbelief, but rather for the purpose of repelling the aggression that the camp of unbelief threatens with, in what is jurisprudentially called 'hirabah' (waging war against society). This was confirmed by the practical stance of Islam through the call of the Prophet - peace be upon him - in its various stages. The other level: The Quran's informative discourse about the other in his eagerness to seduce Muslims by various means, and his insistence on that. But this is restricted to a group of unbelievers, or even to the totality of the unbelievers from the People of the Book specifically in the time of the Prophet - peace be upon him - without that necessarily implying generalization to all opponents, and without that meaning a stance that Islam approves as well. It is as if Islam – after defining its stance on the issue of civilizational push-and-pull in the first level – indicates that the other differs from it in the stance itself. Therefore, if the idea of civilizational push-and-pull becomes inevitable, the sole cause of it is the other party, not Islam. And based on this conclusion, Islamic education does not stand against ideas and trends as an opponent, but rather stands as an interactive equal. But when some of those Western ideas and trends – for example – seek hegemony and attempt to extend influence over other theories, ideas, and trends, as is the case with what is today called modern education, pragmatist education, under the pretext that others are reactionary and obsolete utopias, then the stance of Islamic education shifts from the position of acknowledgment and coexistence with the other to the position of contestation over rightfulness, legitimacy, and the mark of universality. This is supposed to be clearly and directly reflected through the educational philosophy guiding the educational system with all its elements in the Muslim society. It seems that the absence of Islamic educational philosophy from the field of equal competition is due to two main reasons:
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological expansion of the Quranic stance on civilizational interaction. The author begins by referencing a researcher's distinction between 'صراع' (conflict) and 'تدافع' (push-and-pull), where the latter implies pluralism, which is portrayed as a divine custom (سنة). Islam, according to this view, embraces pluralism and coexistence, with a common ground in neutral sciences while civilizations have distinct philosophies. Another researcher is cited who links the seal of prophethood (ختم النبوة) to the end of civilizational cycles, suggesting that while we still experience civilizational cycles, signs of their decline are emerging. The author concludes by proposing a two-level analysis of Quranic discourse on civilizational push-and-pull. The first level is the Quran's self-perception: full recognition of the other's identity and right to establish their own civilization, provided they do not transgress. Here, conflict is not for annihilation but to repel aggression (حرابة). The second level is the Quran's informative discourse about the other's attempts to seduce Muslims, but this is context-specific to certain groups in the Prophet's time, not a universal judgment. The author argues that if civilizational push-and-pull becomes necessary, it is initiated by the other, not Islam. Consequently, Islamic education should be interactive and equal, but it shifts to contestation when faced with hegemonic Western ideas like pragmatism. The passage ends by noting the absence of Islamic educational philosophy from equal competition, attributed to two reasons (which are cut off in the provided text). The commentary emphasizes that this is not a logical argument but a conceptual distinction and application to educational philosophy.

Distinguishing Sources from Methods in Islamic Epistemology
Author distinguishes 'sources' from 'methods' in Islamic epistemology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'sources' (masādir) and 'methods/means' (ṭarāʾiq/wasā'il) of knowledge as defined by the author, and identify the different positions in the debate.

Core Idea
The source is the origin; the method is the instrument—conflation causes conceptual confusion.

In Islamic epistemology, knowing where knowledge comes from (source) and how we access it (method) is vital. This passage surveys several scholarly positions and clarifies the author's own terminology. By parsing this discussion, you will gain a precise tool for analyzing epistemic claims in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Define the key terms in context
Identify the Arabic terms masādir (sources), ṭarāʾiq (methods), and wasā'il (means) as they appear. Note that the author stresses that 'source' (maṣdar) means 'origin' (aṣl), while methods are the instruments used to acquire knowledge.
The author explicitly says: 'مصدر الشيء أصله' — the source of a thing is its origin.
2
Identify the Islamic anchor
Find where the author grounds the ultimate source in divine revelation: 'عَلَّمَ الْإِنْسَانَ مَا لَمْ يَعْلَمْ' (al-Alaq 96:5). Even reason and sense are only operative because God enables them.
This sets Islamic epistemology apart from philosophical conceptions that lack a clear Creator-creature relationship.
3
Map the different researcher positions
List the four views presented: (1) a researcher who equates sources with methods; (2) a reference to Ibn Taymiyya's integration model that lists revelation, reason, and senses as sources; (3) a view that reason is both source (for innate knowledge) and means; (4) a dissenting view that reason is only a faculty of action, not a source.
The author presents these to show the existing terminological confusion.
4
Understand the author's own resolution
The author announces that in this study, 'sources' will mean the principles (al-uṣūl) from which knowledge originates, distinct from the methods/means of acquisition. This is a deliberate choice to avoid ambiguity.
The passage ends with 'الأصول التي...' indicating the definition continues.

وعلى هذا درج بعض الباحثين في استخدامهم للمصادر في سياق الحديث عن الطرائق والعكس، كما تلاحظ ذلك عند أحدهم حين عرض لمنهج (ابن تيمية في النظام المعرفي، فوصفه بالتكامل بين مصادر المعرفة الوحي والعقل والحواس» (١). وفي موطن لاحق عبر بالمصادر والوسائل معاً بوصفهما اسمين لمسمى واحد فقال: ((إن إسقاط أي مصدر أو وسيلة من وسائل المعرفة (الوحي أو العقل أو (الحس أو جعله بديلاً أو مقابلاً لغيره سوف يلحق بالمعرفة كثيراً من الإصابات الفكرية (٢) . ولكن ثمة من يرى فرقًا بين المصطلحين، فلا يمكن إجمال المصادر والطرائق في سياق واحد، بحجة أن العقل - مثلاً - هو وحده الذي يمكن أن يكون مصدراً للمعرفة ووسيلة لها بآن واحد لأنه مصدر للمعارف الغريزية أو الفطرية المركوزة في الإنسان، ووسيلة نحصل بواسطتها على المعلومة من المصدر، بالفكر والنظر الطويل(۳).

First: Some researchers' conception of the term. One researcher indicates: Philosophy uses (sources of knowledge) and means by it methods or means of knowledge, but the term 'sources of knowledge' in the philosophical concept is not what is meant by its methods in our precise conception. That is because the source of a thing is its origin, and the origin of knowledge with us is divine: 'He taught man what he did not know' [Surah al-Alaq 96:5]. Even our use of tools and means of knowledge—reason and sense—is only by God's enabling and empowering these means to perform their cognitive role. Meanwhile, the relationship of man to his Creator is not clear in the philosophical concept, and therefore their methods of knowledge are their sources and origins together (1). The truth is that this is not purely philosophical usage, for here is Imam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali (d. 505 AH) who exposed the incoherence of the divine philosophers (metaphysicians) as never before, and I do not think anyone after him will approach his mastery of philosophical investigations, as attested by his book 'The Incoherence of the Philosophers' and 'The Deliverer from Error.' And yet we find him deeming reason as the fountainhead, source, and foundation of knowledge, saying: 'Reason is the fountainhead of knowledge, its source and foundation; knowledge flows from it as fruit from the tree, light from the sun, and sight from the eye' (2). Likewise, one researcher explains 'source' as meaning the fountainhead, origin, and gateway from which we reach these cognitions (3). Upon this, some researchers proceeded in their use of 'sources' in the context of discussing methods, and vice versa, as you notice with one of them when he presented the methodology of Ibn Taymiyya in the cognitive system, describing it as integration between the sources of knowledge: revelation, reason, and senses (1). And in a later place, he used 'sources' and 'means' together as two names for one referent, saying: 'Eliminating any source or means of knowledge (revelation, reason, or sense) or making it an alternative or counterpart to another will cause much intellectual damage to knowledge' (2). However, there is one who sees a difference between the two terms, so that sources and methods cannot be summarized in one context, arguing that reason, for example, alone can be a source of knowledge and a means at the same time, because it is a source for instinctive or innate cognitions ingrained in man, and a means by which we obtain information from the source, through thinking and prolonged contemplation (3). Another researcher disagrees, considering that the cognitions of revelation and the universe are only arrived at through the act of reflection, deliberation, and reasoning, and that reason is not a limb in man but rather an energy, ability, and action. It appears in the Qur'an only in verb form, not noun form (4). And he asks disapprovingly: If reason is a source of knowledge and its fountainhead, then what is the means by which knowledge is extracted or attained from this source? (5). And to escape the ambiguity that may lead to lack of clear determination of what is meant by the terms 'sources' and 'methods' in this study, objective necessity requires promptly clarifying that, regardless of what other researchers intend by their use of the terms 'sources' and 'methods,' what is meant here by their use is: the principles that...

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the terminology 'sources of knowledge' versus 'methods/means of knowledge.' The author begins by presenting a researcher who criticizes the philosophical use of 'sources of knowledge' to mean methods, arguing that in Islamic thought, the source (asl) is the origin, and the ultimate source is divine revelation. Means like reason and sense are tools enabled by God. The author then cites al-Ghazali, who despite his critique of philosophers, still calls reason the fountainhead of knowledge, showing that the philosophical distinction is not absolute. The author then presents other researchers: one conflates sources and means; another distinguishes them by noting that reason can be both source and means; a third denies reason as a source and emphasizes that it is a faculty of action. After surveying these views, the author concludes that to avoid confusion, in this study 'sources' and 'methods' will refer to the principles (or origins) of knowledge. The passage thus serves to clarify the author's own terminology and to situate it within the broader debate.

In Islamic epistemology, a 'source' (maṣdar) is the origin from which knowledge springs (ultimately divine), while a 'method' (ṭarīqa) is the instrument enabled by God for acquiring it; maintaining this distinction prevents conceptual errors.

Hearing as a Fundamental Means of Learning
Author cites Quranic verses to establish hearing's primacy in knowledge acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic evidence used to argue for the primacy of hearing over sight in knowledge acquisition.

Core Idea
Hearing is the primary channel for divine knowledge, as shown by Quranic rebukes of blocking ears.

In this passage, the author builds an epistemological case for the centrality of hearing (sam‘) in learning, using four Quranic verses. The disbelievers' deliberate blocking of their ears indicates that hearing is the gateway for divine guidance. As you read, notice how each verse reinforces the same point: rejection of truth is associated with refusing to hear. This lesson will help you recognize the pattern and its logical strength.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's central claim
Read the final sentence of the passage. What is the author asserting about hearing vs sight? He claims hearing is a fundamental means of learning and even surpasses sight in importance.
2
Locate the Quranic verses cited
The passage quotes verses from Surah Nuh, al-Jathiya, Luqman, and Fussilat. List them and note what action the disbelievers take with their ears in each verse.
The verses describe: (1) putting fingers in ears, (2) acting as if they did not hear, (3) having deafness in ears, (4) being told not to listen.
3
Analyze how each verse supports the claim
For each verse, ask: why does the author choose this verse? All verses show that turning away from hearing is a sign of rejection. This implies that hearing is the key channel through which divine guidance comes.
The author uses the prohibition of listening (فصلت) as evidence that hearing is so critical that enemies try to prevent it.
4
Assess the strength of the cumulative argument
Consider why the author uses multiple verses. Is the argument stronger because several verses point to the same conclusion? The author treats these verses as collectively (في مجموعه) indicative of a general principle.

Understanding Nazar: Sensory Sight and Intellectual Insight
Author clarifies that 'nazar' includes both sensory and intellectual perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a Quranic usage of 'nazar' refers to sensory sight, intellectual reflection, or both, based on Raghib's definition.

Core Idea
Nazar in the Quran bridges the eye and the mind.

When you read 'afala yanzurun' (do they not look?) in the Quran, are you being asked to merely see or to reflect? The author, citing Raghib al-Isfahani, clarifies that nazar encompasses both turning the eye and turning the insight. This dual meaning is essential for understanding Quranic calls to observe creation — they are not just physical but also intellectual invitations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object of nazar
Look at what the verse asks to be observed. For example, in Surah al-Ghashiya 88:17-21, the objects are camels, sky, mountains, and earth — these are physical entities visible to the eye.
2
Check the context for mental activity
Notice if the verse includes words like 'tafakkur' (reflection), 'tadhakkur' (reminder), or 'i'tibar' (lesson). In 88:21, the verse says 'fa dhakkir' (so remind), indicating that the purpose of looking goes beyond sight to intellectual consideration.
3
Apply Raghib's two-part definition
Raghib defines nazar as 'taqleeb al-basar wa al-basira' (turning the eye and the insight). Determine if the context requires only physical turning of the eye, only mental turning, or both. Often in Quranic calls to observe nature, both are intended.
4
Classify the nazar type
Based on the previous steps, classify the nazar as sensory (only basar), intellectual (only basira), or compound (both). Provide evidence from the verse's wording and broader context.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المواطن التي ورد البصر فيها مستقلاً نظراً لأهميته أيضاً، وما يستتبع ذلك من توظيف لهذه النعمة الموازية لنعمة السمع على النحو المشروع كذلك: أَفَلا يَنظُرُونَ (١) إِلَى الإِبِلِ كَيْفَ خُلِقَتْ، وَإِلَى السَّمَاءِ كَيْفَ رُفِعَتْ وَإِلَى الْجِبَالِ كَيْفَ نُصِبَتْ، وَإِلَى الأَرْضِ كَيْفَ سُطِحَتْ، فَذَكِّرْ إِنَّمَا أَنتَ مُذَكِّرٌ [الغاشية: ۱۷/۸۸-۲۱]. (۱) قد تشعر هذه الآيات التي ورد لفظها بـ (نظر) ومشتقاتها أن النظر المقصود فيها غير النظر الحسي، وهنا لابد من الإفصاح عن أنها حقاً قد تشتمل على هذا المعنى، وهو المسمى بـ (النظر العقلي) وسنأتي، إليه عند الحديث عن المصادر والطرائق العقلية - إن شاء الله تعالى – ولكن تم توخي الآيات الدالة على النظر الحسي هنا، وإن كانت لربما تضم العقلي أيضاً – كما سبق - ، غير أن ذلك يعني عدم دلالتها الحسية بقدر ما يعني عمق العلاقة بين الحسي والعقلي. وقد عرف الراغب الأصفهاني النظر بالأمرين معا فقال: (( النظر تقليب البصر والبصيرة لإدراك الشيء ورؤيته، وقد يراد به التأمل والفحص، وقد يراد به المعرفة الحاصلة بعد الفحص وهو الروية، يقال نظرت فلم تنظر أي لم تتأمل ولم تتروّ)) (المفردات في غريب القرآن، ٤٩٧).
And among the places where sight appears independently, given its importance as well, and what follows from that of proper utilization of this blessing parallel to the blessing of hearing in the prescribed manner, is: 'Do they not look at the camels how they are created, and at the sky how it is raised, and at the mountains how they are set up, and at the earth how it is spread out? So remind, for you are only a reminder' [al-Ghashiya 88:17-21]. (1) These verses in which the word 'nazar' and its derivatives appear may suggest that the intended nazar is not sensory nazar, and here it is necessary to clarify that they indeed may include this meaning, which is called 'intellectual nazar', and we will come to it when discussing rational sources and methods – if Allah wills – but the verses indicating sensory nazar were sought here, although they perhaps also include the intellectual – as before – but that does not mean their sensory indication is diminished, rather it indicates the depth of the relationship between the sensory and the intellectual. And al-Raghib al-Isfahani defined nazar with both together, saying: 'Nazar is the turning of the eye and the insight to perceive and see something, and it may mean reflection and examination, and it may mean the knowledge resulting after examination, which is deliberation. It is said: "I looked but did not nazar" meaning you did not reflect and did not deliberate.' (al-Mufradat fi Gharib al-Quran, p. 497).
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a definitional note on the term 'nazar' (نظر). The author begins by introducing the topic of sight (basar) as an independent faculty, citing the Qur'anic verse from Surah al-Ghashiya that calls for looking at creation. He then enters a lengthy footnote to clarify the term 'nazar' used in these verses. He explains that 'nazar' can be either sensory (physical sight) or intellectual (mental reflection). Although he chose verses that emphasize the sensory aspect, he acknowledges that they may also include the intellectual. He then quotes al-Raghib al-Isfahani's definition, which combines both: nazar is the turning of the eye (sight) and the insight (mind) to perceive something. It can mean physical sight, reflection, or the knowledge resulting from reflection.

Sight as a Means of Learning: Quranic Evidence and Ranking
Collects Quranic verses on sight and ranks it below hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze how Quranic verses establish the epistemological role of sight and defend the author's ranking of hearing as superior.

Core Idea
Sight is a door to knowledge, but hearing is the master key in Quranic epistemology.

This passage presents a collection of Quranic verses that command or encourage sight to acquire knowledge about Allah, creation, and false deities. However, the author concludes with a ranking: sight is important but less effective than hearing. This lesson will walk you through the verses and the comparative argument, helping you understand how Islamic epistemology prioritizes hearing based on divine evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's thesis on sight
Find where the author explicitly states that sight is important for learning but ranks it below hearing. This thesis governs the entire passage.
Look for the concluding sentence: 'sight comes in a degree after hearing in effectiveness.'
2
List the Quranic verses cited for sight
Read each verse sequentially: Yunus 10:101, al-Qasas 28:72, al-Dhariyat 51:21, Ghafir 40:81, Fatir 35:40, Luqman 31:11, al-Zukhruf 43:19. Note the command or rhetorical question involving 'see', 'look', or 'show'.
Each verse is a distinct call to use physical sight as a means of reflection.
3
Analyze the purpose of each verse
For each verse, determine what specific knowledge it aims to produce: knowledge of divine creation, signs, self, or the impotence of false gods. This shows the range of sight's utility.
For example, 'And in yourselves, do you not see?' directs sight inward to reflect on human creation.
4
Understand the comparative ranking
The author explicitly states that sight is less effective than hearing. Infer why this might be: hearing is more directly linked to revelation (Quran and hadith) which is primarily auditory, while sight is indirect and easier to misinterpret.
The author does not elaborate reasons here, but the ranking is clear.
5
Apply the reasoning to a new verse
Take another Quranic verse that commands sight (e.g., al-A'raf 7:185: 'Do they not look at the dominion of the heavens and the earth?') and explain how it fits the pattern, then argue why hearing still trumps sight in Islamic epistemology.
This step tests your understanding of the epistemological hierarchy.

Hearing and Sight Paired in the Quran
Author presents Quranic verses pairing hearing and sight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Quranic verses that pair hearing and sight and explain their epistemological significance for receiving guidance.

Core Idea
Hearing and sight are paired to show their combined necessity for faith.

The Quran often pairs hearing and sight to highlight their complementary role in human perception and accountability. This lesson examines three such verses to understand how blocked senses hinder guidance.

Guided Path
1
Locate the paired terms
In each verse, find where ‘hearing’ (sam‘) and ‘sight’ (basar) or their derivatives appear together.
In Yunus 10:31, they appear as ‘am man yamliku as-sam‘a wal-absara’.
2
Identify the context of blockage
Note if the verse describes these senses as blocked or impaired. In Hud 11:20, the disbelievers ‘could not hear’ and ‘did not see’. In al-Kahf 18:101, their eyes were covered and they could not hear.
3
Observe the pattern across the three verses
All three verses pair hearing and sight in contexts of divine questioning, judgment, or rejection of faith. This shows that both senses must be functional to receive guidance.
4
Extract the epistemological lesson
The pairing implies that guidance requires both auditory reception (hearing the message) and visual perception (observing signs). Blockage of either leads to loss of accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهناك مواطن جُمِعَ فيها بين السمع والبصر : قُلْ مَنْ يَرْزُقُكُمْ مِنَ السَّمَاءِ وَالْأَرْضِ أَمْ مَنْ يَمْلِكُ السَّمْعَ وَالْأَبْصارَ [يونس: ۳۱/۱۰]. يوما كانُوا يَسْتَطِيعُونَ السَّمْعَ وَمَا كَانُوا يُبْصِرُونَ ﴾ [هود: ٢٠/١١]. وَعَرَضْنَا جَهَنَّمَ يَوْمَئِذٍ لِلْكَافِرِينَ عَرْضاً، الَّذِينَ كَانَتْ أَعْيُنُهُمْ فِي غِطَاء عَنْ ذِكْرِي وَكَانُوا لا يَسْتَطِيعُونَ سَمْعاً﴾ [الكهف: ۱۰۰/۱۸-۱۰۱].
And there are places where hearing and sight are combined: 'Say, "Who provides for you from the heaven and the earth? Or who possesses hearing and sight?"' [Yunus 10:31]. 'On the Day they were unable to hear, and they did not see' [Hud 11:20]. 'And We will present Hell on that Day to the disbelievers, plainly, those whose eyes were within a cover from My remembrance, and they were unable to hear' [al-Kahf 18:100-101].

This short passage presents Quranic verses that mention both hearing (sam') and sight (basar) together. The first verse from Yunus asks who controls hearing and sight, emphasizing that Allah is the possessor of these senses. The second from Hud describes the disbelievers on Judgment Day who could neither hear nor see properly. The third from al-Kahf shows that disbelievers had their eyes covered and were unable to hear the Quran. These verses illustrate that hearing and sight are paired in the Quran to highlight their importance in receiving guidance and being accountable.

Qur'anic Meanings of Qalb and Its Relationship to Intellect
Author lists three Qur'anic senses of qalb and discusses fu'ad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three Qur'anic meanings of qalb and explain the linguistic debate about fu'ad.

Core Idea
The Quran uses 'heart' (qalb) for intellect, opinion, and the physical organ—each rooted in revelation.

This lesson examines the rich vocabulary of intellect in the Quran, focusing on the terms qalb and fu'ad. The author highlights that while linguists often equate qalb with 'aql, the Quran itself uses qalb in three distinct senses. Understanding these shades of meaning is crucial for accurate tafsir and for grasping the Quranic epistemology of the heart as a seat of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings of qalb
From the passage, list the three distinct meanings the author attributes to qalb in the Quran: intellect, opinion/planning, and the physical heart in the chest. Note the supporting verses for each.
2
Understand the relationship between qalb and 'aql
The passage states that linguists consider qalb and 'aql as synonyms. Reflect on how this equivalence shapes tafsir traditions.
3
Analyze the debate about fu'ad
The author notes a dispute whether fu'ad is synonymous with qalb or carries a specific nuance. Identify the point of contention and its implications for interpreting verses like Q 17:46.
4
Connect basira to basar
The analogy: just as basar (sight) is complete perception via the eye, basira (insight) is complete perception via the heart. Recognize how this aligns with the functional view of the intellect in Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القرآن يتميز التعبير عن العقل بمفردات متنوعة، فقد عبر عنه بالنهي، وبالحجر، وباللب، وبالبصيرة إلى غير ذلك من الصيغ، ولكن استخدامه للفظي القلب والفؤاد أكثر ما يلفت النظر. ومع أن اللغويين يعدون القلب هو العقل والعقل هو القلب، وأن المعقول ما تعقله بقلبك؛ فإن ثمة نزاعاً حول معنى الفؤاد من حيث الخصوصية هل هي له أم للقلب؟
ويرد القلب في القرآن بمعنى من المعاني الثلاثة التالية: أولها: العقل وذلك في مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ فِي ذَلِكَ لَذِكْرَى لِمَنْ كَانَ لَهُ قَلْب﴾ [ق: ٣٧/٥٠].
الثاني: بمعنى الرأي والتدبير، وذلك في مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿تَحْسَبُهُمْ جَمِيعاً وَقُلُوبُهُمْ شَتّى﴾ [الحشر: ١٤/٥٩].
الثالث: بمعنى حقيقة القلب الذي في الصدر، وذلك في مثل قوله تعالى: ... وَلَكِنْ تَعْمَى الْقُلُوبُ الَّتِي فِي الصُّدُور﴾ [الحج: ٤٦/٢٢].
وهذا المعنى الثالث إنما يشير إلى الجانب الوظيفي للعقل. فكما أن البصر اسم للإدراك التام الكامل الحاصل بالعين التي في الرأس؛ فالبصيرة اسم للإدراك التام الحاصل في القلب. قال تعالى: ﴿ بَلِ الإِنْسَانُ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ بَصِيرَةٌ ﴾ [القيامة: ١٤/٧٥]، أي له بنفسه معرفة تامة. أي أن العقل يستخدم في القرآن باعتباره موضعاً للعلم والمعرفة وَجَعَلْنا عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ أَكِنَّةً أَنْ يَفْقَهُوهُ وَفِي آذانِهِمْ وَقْراً وَإِذَا ذَكَرْتَ رَبَّكَ فِي الْقُرْآنَ وَحْدَهُ وَلَّوْا عَلَى أَدْبَارِهِمْ نُفُوراً [الإسراء: ٤٦/١٧].
And in the Quran, the expression for the intellect is distinguished by diverse vocabulary; it has been expressed by 'al-nuha', 'al-hijr', 'al-lubb', 'al-basira' and other forms. But its use of the two terms 'al-qalb' and 'al-fu'ad' is most striking. And although linguists consider the heart (qalb) to be the intellect, and the intellect to be the heart, and that the intelligible is what you comprehend with your heart, yet there is a dispute concerning the meaning of 'al-fu'ad' specifically: is it the same as the heart or not?
And the heart (qalb) occurs in the Quran in one of the following three meanings: First: the intellect, as in His saying 'Indeed in that is a reminder for whoever has a heart' [Qaf 50:37]. Second: meaning opinion and planning, as in His saying 'You think they are united, but their hearts are divided' [al-Hashr 59:14]. Third: meaning the actual heart in the chest, as in His saying '... but blind are the hearts that are in the chests' [al-Hajj 22:46].
This third meaning indicates the functional aspect of the intellect. For just as 'sight' (al-basar) is the name for the complete perception obtained through the eye in the head, so 'insight' (al-basira) is the name for the complete perception obtained in the heart. He says 'Rather, man is a witness against himself' [al-Qiyama 75:14], meaning he has complete knowledge of himself. That is, the intellect is used in the Quran as a place of knowledge and cognition: 'And We have placed over their hearts coverings lest they understand it, and in their ears deafness, and when you mention your Lord alone in the Quran, they turn away in aversion' [al-Isra 17:46].
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage examines the Quranic vocabulary for intellect, noting that while multiple terms are used (nuha, hijr, lubb, basira), the most prominent are 'qalb' (heart) and 'fu'ad'. It highlights a linguistic debate over whether 'fu'ad' is synonymous with 'qalb' or has a distinct meaning. The author then enumerates three distinct meanings of 'qalb' in the Quran: (1) the intellect itself, as in Q 50:37; (2) opinion and inner counsel, as in Q 59:14; (3) the physical heart in the chest, as in Q 22:46. The third meaning is linked to a functional view of the intellect: just as 'basar' (sight) refers to perception via the eye, 'basira' (insight) refers to perception via the heart. The passage concludes by asserting that the intellect in the Quran is the locus of knowledge, citing Q 17:46 about veils over hearts preventing understanding. This presentation avoids equating intellect with the brain and instead roots it in the heart as a spiritual center of cognition.

Key Terms
القلب
الفؤاد
البصيرة
البصر
العقل

Checkpoint

A) The intellect
B) Opinion and planning
C) The physical heart in the chest
D) The emotions and feelings

Key Takeaway
The Quranic term qalb has three distinct meanings—intellect, opinion, and the physical organ—and recognizing them is essential for accurate tafsir and understanding the Quran's epistemology of the heart.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Quranic Critique of Blind Imitation and the Call for Reason
Quranic critique of ancestral authority and call for reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic arguments against ancestral authority and explain how the Quran calls for the use of reason ('aql) in matters of belief and practice.

Core Idea
The Quran rejects blind taqlid to ancestors and demands reasoning based on divine guidance.

This passage from a modern Islamic scholar examines the Quran's repeated condemnation of those who blindly follow the traditions of their forefathers without evidence. The Quran does not reject all tradition, but it clearly calls for the use of reason to distinguish truth from falsehood. In this lesson, you will learn to recognize the pattern of Quranic critique and its epistemological foundation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of criticism
Look for phrases like 'sufficient for us is that upon which we found our fathers' and 'we follow the ways of our fathers'. The Quran targets those who reject revelation and reason in favor of inherited custom.
Note that the criticism is not of all tradition, but of tradition that contradicts revelation and leads to falsehood.
2
Extract the Quranic arguments against blind imitation
Find verses that point out that the ancestors had no knowledge or guidance (لا يعلمون شيئًا ولا يهتدون), that they followed conjecture (الظن) and desire (الهوى), and that their practices have no divine authority (ما أنزل الله بها من سلطان).
The Quran often uses rhetorical questions (أَوَلَوْ) to challenge the logic of blind following.
3
Recognize the positive call for reason
Notice that the Quran contrasts imitation with the use of reason (يعقلون، يعلمون). The verses affirm that lawful and unlawful are determined by Allah alone, and that believers must use their intellect to follow divine guidance.
The passage highlights that the Quran seeks to liberate the 'aql from the dominance of authoritarian thinking.
4
Apply to a contemporary scenario
Consider how this Quranic principle applies when someone today insists on a practice simply because 'our ancestors did it', without evidence from revelation or reason. The Quran requires us to question such claims.
This does not mean rejecting all heritage, but evaluating it by the standards of truth and justice.

الباب الثالث: الفصل الثاني - العقل
فَوَمَا جَعَلَ اللهُ مِنْ بَحِيرَةٍ وَلا سَائِبَةٍ وَلا وَصِيلَةٍ وَلَا حَامِ وَلَكِنَّ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا يَفْتَرُونَ عَلَى اللهِ الْكَذِبَ وَأَكْثَرُهُمْ لا يَعْقِلُونَ، وَإِذَا قِيلَ لَهُمْ تَعَالَوْا إِلَى مَا أَنْزَلَ اللَّهُ وَإِلَى الرَّسُولِ قَالُوا حَسْبُنا ما وَجَدْنَا عَلَيْهِ آبَاءَنَا أَوَلَوْ كَانَ آباؤُهُمْ لا يَعْلَمُونَ شَيْئًا وَلَا يَهْتَدُونَ ﴾ [المائدة: ١٠٣/٥-١٠٤].
إن مسائل الحل والحرمة بوجه خاص، وإصدار الأحكام بعامة ليست من حق آبائهم، تماماً كما ليست من صلاحية أي مراكز تفكير سلطوية، لأن ذلك ضرب من الشرك في توحيد الألوهية:
﴿قُلْ مَنْ حَرَّمَ زِينَةَ اللَّهِ الَّتِي أَخْرَجَ لِعِبَادِهِ وَالطَّيِّبَاتِ مِنَ الرِّزْقِ قُلْ هِيَ لِلَّذِينَ آمَنُوا فِي الْحَيَاةِ الدُّنْيا خالِصَةً يَوْمَ الْقِيَامَةِ كَذَلِكَ نُفَصِّلُ الآيَاتِ لِقَوْمٍ يَعْلَمُونَ، قُلْ إنّما حَرَّمَ رَبِّيَ الْفَوَاحِشَ مَا ظَهَرَ مِنْهَا وَمَا بَطَنَ وَالإِثْمَ وَالْبَغْيَ بغَيْرِ الْحَقِّ وَأَن تُشْرِكُوا بِاللهِ ما لَمْ يُنَزِّلَ بِهِ سُلْطَاناً وأن تَقُولُوا عَلَى اللهِ ما لا تَعْلَمُونَ [الأعراف: ۳۲/۷ - ۳۳].
قُلْ أَرَأَيْتُمْ ما أَنْزَلَ اللَّهُ لَكُمْ مِنْ رِزْقٍ فَجَعَلْتُمْ مِنْهُ حَرَامًا وَحَلَالًا قُلْ اللَّهُ أَذِنَ لَكُمْ أَمْ عَلَى اللَّهِ تَفْتَرُونَ﴾ [يونس: ٥٩/١٠].
وَيَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِنَّ كَثِيراً مِنَ الأَخْبَارِ وَالرُّهْبَانِ لَيَأْكُلُونَ أَمْوالَ النَّاسِ بِالْباطِلِ وَيَصُدُّونَ عَنْ سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ وَالَّذِينَ يَكْنِزُونَ الذَّهَبَ وَالْفِضَّةَ وَلَا يُنْفِقُونَها فِي سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ فَبَشِّرْهُمْ بِعَذَابٍ أَلِيمٍ﴾ [التوبة: ٣٤/٩].
بل إن سلطة الآباء حين تتعدى حقوقها لتدعى أحقية الطاعة المطلقة، حتى في الإشراك بالله فلا مناص - في هذه الحال - من التحرر من سلطتها في ذلك:
﴿وَإِنْ جاهَدَاكَ عَلَى أَنْ تُشْرِكَ بِي مَا لَيْسَ لَكَ بِهِ عِلْمٌ فَلا تُطِعْهُما وَصاحِبْهُما فِي الدُّنْيا مَعْرُوفًا وَاتَّبِعْ سَبِيلَ مَنْ أَتَابَ إِلَيَّ ثُمَّ إِلَيَّ مَرْجِعُكُمْ فَأُنَبِّئُكُمْ بِمَا كُنتُمْ تَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [لقمان: ١٥/٣١].
وفي سياقات أخرى كثيرة يذم القرآن الكريم النزعة الآبائية، جاهداً ويسعى خلال تسفيه معتقدات الآباء و موروثاتهم المختلفة - إلى تحرير العقل من سطوة تفكير السلطات ومراكزها وإيقاظه ابتغاء معاودة نشاطه وفاعليته، ومما جاء في هذا:
وَإِذا قِيلَ لَهُمُ اتَّبِعُوا مَا أَنْزَلَ اللهُ قَالُوا بَلْ نَتَّبِعُ مَا وَجَدْنَا عَلَيْهِ آبَاءَنَا أَوَلَوْ كانَ الشَّيْطَانُ يَدْعُوهُمْ إِلَى عَذَابِ السَّعِيرِ﴾ [لقمان: ٢١/٣١].
بَلْ قالُوا إِنَّا وَجَدْنَا آبَاءَنَا عَلَى أُمَّةٍ وَإِنَّا عَلَى آثَارِهِمْ مُهْتَدُونَ، وَكَذَلِكَ مَا أَرْسَلْنَا مِنْ قَبْلِكَ فِي قَرْيَةٍ مِنْ نَذِيرٍ إِلَّا قَالَ مُتْرَفُوهَا إِنَّا وَجَدْنَا آبَاءَنَا عَلَى أُمَّةٍ وَإِنَّا عَلَى آثَارِهِمْ مُقْتَدُونَ، قَالَ أَوَلَوْ جِئْتُكُمْ بِأَهْدَى مِمَّا وَجَدْتُمْ عَلَيْهِ آبَاءَكُمْ قَالُوا إِنَّا بِما أُرْسِلْتُمْ بِهِ كَافِرُونَ ﴾ [الزخرف: ٢٢/٤٣-٢٤].
إِنَّهُمْ أَلْفَوْا آبَاءَهُمْ ضَالِّينَ، فَهُمْ عَلَى آثَارِهِمْ يُهْرَعُونَ ﴾ [الصافات: ٦٩/٣٧-٧٠].
ويشن حملة عنيفة على معبوداتهم بأسمائها، حيث لا مستند لشرعيتها إلا وصف الآباء لها، هذا إلى جانب اتباعهم الظن والهوى، مع أن مصدر الهداية قائم في القرآن الكريم ﴿أَفَرَأَيْتُمُ اللَّاتَ وَالْعُزَّى، وَمَنَاةَ الثَّالِثَةَ الأُخْرَى، أَلَكُمُ الذَّكَرُ وَلَهُ الأُنثَى، تِلْكَ إِذًا قِسْمَةٌ ضِيزَى إِنْ هِيَ إِلَّا أَسْمَاءً سَمَّيْتُمُوهَا أَنْتُمْ وَآبَاؤُكُمْ مَا أَنْزَلَ اللهُ بِهَا مِنْ سُلْطَانٍ إِنْ يَتَّبِعُونَ إِلَّا الظَّنَّ وَمَا تَهْوَى الأَنْفُسُ وَلَقَدْ جَاءَهُمْ مِنْ رَبِّهِمُ الْهُدَى ﴾ [النجم: ١٩/٥٣-٢٣].
ويؤكد القرآن نفيه الصريح لأي قدرة مادية أو عقلية لدى معبوداتهم تلك، فكيف تستقيم هذه العبادة مع أن فاقد الشيء لا يعطيه:
وَاتَّخَذُوا مِنْ دُونِ اللَّهِ شُفَعَاءَ قُلْ أَوَلَوْ كَانُوا لَا يَمْلِكُونَ شَيْئًا وَلَا يَعْقِلُونَ ﴾ [الزمر: ٤٣/٣٩].
Chapter Three: Section Two - The Intellect
And Allah has not appointed [such innovations as] bahirah or sa'ibah or wasilah or ham; but those who disbelieve invent lies against Allah, and most of them do not reason. And when it is said to them, 'Come to what Allah has revealed and to the Messenger,' they say, 'Sufficient for us is that upon which we found our fathers.' Even though their fathers knew nothing, nor were they guided? [Quran 5:103-104] Indeed, matters of lawful and unlawful in particular, and issuing rulings in general, are not the right of their fathers, just as they are not within the authority of any power-centered thought centers, because that is a kind of polytheism in the oneness of divinity: Say, 'Who has forbidden the adornment of Allah which He has produced for His servants and the good [things] of provision?' Say, 'They are for those who believe during the life of this world, exclusively for them on the Day of Resurrection.' Thus do We detail the verses for a people who know. Say, 'My Lord has only forbidden immoralities – what is apparent of them and what is concealed – and sin, and oppression without right, and that you associate with Allah that for which He has not sent down authority, and that you say about Allah that which you do not know.' [Quran 7:32-33] Say, 'Have you seen what Allah has sent down to you of provision, of which you have made [some] lawful and [some] unlawful?' Say, 'Has Allah permitted you [to do so], or do you invent lies about Allah?' [Quran 10:59] O you who have believed, indeed many of the scholars and monks devour the wealth of people unjustly and avert [them] from the way of Allah. And those who hoard gold and silver and spend it not in the way of Allah – give them tidings of a painful punishment. [Quran 9:34] Rather, when the authority of fathers exceeds its rights, claiming the right of absolute obedience, even in associating partners with Allah, then there is no escape – in this case – from liberation from their authority in that: And if they strive to make you associate with Me that of which you have no knowledge, do not obey them but accompany them in [this] world with appropriate kindness and follow the way of those who turn back to Me [in repentance]. Then to Me is your return, and I will inform you about what you used to do. [Quran 31:15] And in many other contexts, the Noble Quran condemns the ancestral tendency, striving and seeking, through debasing the beliefs of the fathers and their various inheritances, to liberate the intellect from the dominance of the thinking of authorities and their centers and to awaken it seeking the resumption of its activity and effectiveness. Among what has come in this regard: And when it is said to them, 'Follow what Allah has revealed,' they say, 'Rather, we will follow that upon which we found our fathers.' Even if Satan was inviting them to the punishment of the Blaze? [Quran 31:21] But they say, 'Indeed, we found our fathers upon a religion, and we are, in their footsteps, righteously guided.' And similarly, We did not send before you any warner into a city except that its affluent said, 'Indeed, we found our fathers upon a religion, and we are, in their footsteps, following.' [The prophet] said, 'Even if I brought you better guidance than that upon which you found your fathers?' They said, 'Indeed we, in that with which you have been sent, are disbelievers.' [Quran 43:22-24] Indeed, they found their fathers astray, so they are hastening in their footsteps. [Quran 37:69-70] And it launches a violent campaign against their objects of worship by name, where there is no basis for their legitimacy except the description of the fathers for them, along with their following of conjecture and desire, even though the source of guidance is established in the Noble Quran: So have you considered al-Lat and al-'Uzza, and Manat, the third, the other? Is the male for you and for Him the female? That, then, is an unjust division. They are not but names which you have named – you and your fathers – for which Allah has sent down no authority. They follow not except assumption and what [their] souls desire, and there has already come to them from their Lord guidance. [Quran 53:19-23] And the Quran emphasizes its explicit denial of any material or intellectual ability for those objects of worship, so how can this worship be valid when one who lacks a thing cannot give it: And they have taken besides Allah intercessors. Say, 'Even though they do not possess anything, nor do they reason?' [Quran 39:43]
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological critique by the author using Quranic verses to argue against blind imitation of ancestors and for the use of reason. It opens with a Quranic statement that Allah did not institute certain pagan practices (like bahirah, sa'ibah) and that disbelievers invent lies, with most of them not using reason (لا يعقلون). The author then draws a lesson: matters of lawful and unlawful are not the right of ancestors or any authoritarian centers of thought, as that constitutes shirk in the oneness of divinity. Several verses are cited: 1) Quran 7:32-33 establishes that Allah alone forbids immoralities and that one must not speak about Allah without knowledge. 2) Quran 10:59 questions the authority to declare things lawful or unlawful without divine permission. 3) Quran 9:34 condemns religious leaders who devour wealth and obstruct Allah's path. 4) Quran 31:15 commands disobedience to parents if they demand shirk, but kind treatment in worldly matters. The author then generalises: the Quran repeatedly condemns the ancestral tendency (النزعة الآبائية) and aims to liberate the intellect from the dominance of authority, using verses like Quran 31:21 (they follow fathers even if Satan leads to hell), Quran 43:22-24 (they reject better guidance if it contradicts ancestors), and Quran 37:69-70 (they follow their fathers in error). The passage culminates with a critique of idolatry: the idols have no basis except ancestral naming (Quran 53:19-23), and they lack any power or intellect, so how can they be intercessors (Quran 39:43)? The overall argument is that reason must be free from uncritical acceptance of tradition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes the Quran's stance toward ancestral traditions?
A) The Quran accepts all ancestral traditions as valid because they have been practiced for generations.
B) The Quran rejects ancestral traditions outright without exception.
C) The Quran condemns following ancestors when it contradicts revelation and reason, even if it requires disobeying parents in matters of shirk.
D) The Quran only criticizes the ancestors of the Quraysh, not general ancestral tradition.

Key Takeaway
The Quran establishes that reason ('aql) must be used to evaluate inherited traditions, and blind taqlid is condemned when it contradicts divine guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find two instances in the passage where the Quran uses a rhetorical question (أَوَلَوْ) to challenge ancestral authority. Write down the question and explain why it is logically compelling.
?
Imagine a contemporary Muslim argues: 'We must follow the madhhab of our fathers because it has been passed down for generations.' Using the Quranic method from this passage, construct a response that distinguishes when following is acceptable and when it is not.
